href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:History_of_Scotland&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><b>Scotland in the Middle Ages</b> concerns the <a href="/wiki/History_of_Scotland" title="History of Scotland">history of Scotland</a> from the departure of the Romans to the adoption of major aspects of the <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a> in the early sixteenth century.\n</p><p>From the fifth century northern Britain was divided into a series of petty kingdoms. Of these the four most important to emerge were the <a href="/wiki/Picts" title="Picts">Picts</a>, the Gaels of <a href="/wiki/D%C3%A1l_Riata" title="D\xc3\xa1l Riata">D\xc3\xa1l Riata</a>, the Britons of <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Strathclyde" title="Kingdom of Strathclyde">Strathclyde</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon" class="mw-redirect" title="Anglo-Saxon">Anglo-Saxon</a> kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Bernicia" title="Bernicia">Bernicia</a>, later taken over by <a href="/wiki/Northumbria" class="mw-redirect" title="Northumbria">Northumbria</a>. After the arrival of the <a href="/wiki/Vikings" title="Vikings">Vikings</a> in the late eighth century, Scandinavian rulers and colonies were established along parts of the coasts and in the islands.\n</p><p>In the ninth century the Scots and Picts <a href="/wiki/Origins_of_Scotland" class="mw-redirect" title="Origins of Scotland">combined</a> under the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Alpin" title="House of Alpin">House of Alpin</a> to form a single <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Alba" title="Kingdom of Alba">Kingdom of Alba</a>, with a Pictish base and dominated by <a href="/wiki/Gaelic_culture" class="mw-redirect" title="Gaelic culture">Gaelic culture</a>. After the reign of <a href="/wiki/King_David_I" class="mw-redirect" title="King David I">King David I</a> in the twelfth century, the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_monarchs" class="mw-redirect" title="Scottish monarchs">Scottish monarchs</a> are best described as <a href="/wiki/Scoto-Norman" title="Scoto-Norman">Scoto-Norman</a>, preferring <a href="/wiki/French_culture" class="mw-redirect" title="French culture">French culture</a> to native <a href="/wiki/Scottish_culture" class="mw-redirect" title="Scottish culture">Scottish culture</a>. <a href="/wiki/Alexander_II_of_Scotland" title="Alexander II of Scotland">Alexander II</a> and his son <a href="/wiki/Alexander_III_of_Scotland" title="Alexander III of Scotland">Alexander III</a>, were able to regain the remainder of the western seaboard, cumulating the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Perth" title="Treaty of Perth">Treaty of Perth</a> with Norway in 1266.\n</p><p>After being invaded and briefly occupied, Scotland re-established its independence from England under figures including <a href="/wiki/William_Wallace" title="William Wallace">William Wallace</a> in the late thirteenth century and <a href="/wiki/Robert_I_of_Scotland" class="mw-redirect" title="Robert I of Scotland">Robert Bruce</a> in the fourteenth century.\n</p><p>In the fifteenth century under the <a href="/wiki/Stewart_Dynasty" class="mw-redirect" title="Stewart Dynasty">Stewart Dynasty</a>, despite a turbulent <a href="/wiki/Political_history_of_Scotland" class="mw-redirect" title="Political history of Scotland">political history</a>, the crown gained greater political control at the expense of independent lords and regained most of its lost territory to approximately the modern borders of the country. However, the <a href="/wiki/Auld_Alliance" title="Auld Alliance">Auld Alliance</a> with France led to the heavy defeat of a Scottish army at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Flodden" title="Battle of Flodden">Battle of Flodden</a> in 1513 and the death of the king <a href="/wiki/James_IV_of_Scotland" title="James IV of Scotland">James IV</a>, which would be followed by a long minority and a period of political instability. Kingship was the major form of government, growing in sophistication in the <a href="/wiki/Late_medieval_Scotland" class="mw-redirect" title="Late medieval Scotland">late Middle Ages</a>. The scale and nature of war also changed, with larger armies, naval forces and the development of artillery and fortifications. \n</p><p>Christianity in Scotland always <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church_in_Scotland" title="Catholic Church in Scotland">church accepted papal authority</a> (contrary to the implications of <a href="/wiki/Celtic_Christianity" title="Celtic Christianity">Celtic Christianity</a>), introduced monasticism, and from the eleventh century embraced monastic reform, developing a flourishing religious culture that asserted its independence from English control. \n</p><p>Scotland grew from its base in the eastern <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Lowlands" title="Scottish Lowlands">Lowlands</a>, to approximately its modern borders. The varied and dramatic <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_Scotland" title="Geography of Scotland">geography of the land</a> provided a protection against invasion, but limited central control. It also defined the largely pastoral economy, with the first <a href="/wiki/Burgh" title="Burgh">burghs</a> being created from the twelfth century. The population may have grown to a peak of a million before the arrival of the <a href="/wiki/Black_Death" title="Black Death">Black Death</a> in 1337. In the early Middle Ages society was divided between a small aristocracy and larger numbers of freemen and slaves. Serfdom disappeared in the fourteenth century and there was a growth of new social groups.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Pictish" class="mw-redirect" title="Pictish">Pictish</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cumbric" title="Cumbric">Cumbric</a> languages were replaced by <a href="/wiki/Gaelic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Gaelic language">Gaelic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">Old English</a> and later <a href="/wiki/Old_Norse" title="Old Norse">Norse</a>, with Gaelic emerging as the major cultural language. From the eleventh century French was adopted in the court and in the late Middle Ages, <a href="/wiki/Scots_language" title="Scots language">Scots</a>, derived from Old English, became dominant, with Gaelic largely confined to the Highlands. Christianity brought Latin, written culture and monasteries as centres of learning. From the twelfth century, educational opportunities widened and a growth of lay education cumulated in the <a href="/wiki/Education_Act_1496" title="Education Act 1496">Education Act 1496</a>. Until in the fifteenth century, when Scotland gained three universities, Scots pursuing higher education had to travel to England or the continent, where some gained an international reputation. Literature survives in all the major languages present in the early Middle Ages, with Scots emerging as a major literary language from <a href="/wiki/John_Barbour_(poet)" title="John Barbour (poet)">John Barbour</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Brus" title="The Brus">Brus</a></i> (1375), developing a culture of poetry by court <a href="/wiki/Makars" class="mw-redirect" title="Makars">makars</a>, and later major works of prose. Art from the early Middle Ages survives in carving, in metalwork, and elaborate illuminated books, which contributed to the development of the wider <a href="/wiki/Insular_art" title="Insular art">insular style</a>. Much of the finest later work has not survived, but there are a few key examples, particularly of work commissioned in the Netherlands. Scotland had a musical tradition, with secular music composed and performed by <a href="/wiki/Bard" title="Bard">bards</a> and from the thirteenth century, church music increasingly influenced by continental and English forms.\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div class="toctitle" Kingdom of Alba</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-5"><a href="#High_Middle_Ages"><span class="tocnumber">1.2</span> <span class="toctext">High Middle II to Alexander I</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-7"><a href="#Scoto-Norman_kings:_David_I_to_Alexander_III"><span class="tocnumber">1.2.2</span> <span class="toctext">Scoto-Norman Margaret to David II</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-10"><a href="#The_Stewarts:_Robert_II_to_James_IV"><span class="tocnumber">1.3.2</span> <span class="toctext">The Stewarts: Robert II to James IV</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-11"><a href="#Government"><span class="tocnumber">2</span> <span class="toctext">Government</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-12"><a href="#Warfare"><span class="tocnumber">3</span> <span class="toctext">Warfare</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-13"><a href="#Religion"><span class="tocnumber">4</span> <span class="toctext">Religion</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-14"><a href="#Geography"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">Geography</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-15"><a href="#Economy_and_society"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">Economy and society</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-16"><a href="#Economy"><span class="tocnumber">6.1</span> <span class="toctext">Economy</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-17"><a href="#Demography"><span class="tocnumber">6.2</span> <span class="toctext">Demography</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-18"><a href="#Social_structure"><span class="tocnumber">6.3</span> <span class="toctext">Social structure</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-19"><a href="#Culture"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">Culture</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-20"><a href="#Language_and_culture"><span class="tocnumber">7.1</span> <span class="toctext">Language and culture</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-21"><a href="#Education"><span class="tocnumber">7.2</span> <span class="toctext">Education</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-22"><a href="#Literature"><span class="tocnumber">7.3</span> <span class="toctext">Literature</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-23"><a href="#Art"><span class="tocnumber">7.4</span> <span class="toctext">Art</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-24"><a href="#Architecture"><span class="tocnumber">7.5</span> <span class="toctext">Architecture</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-25"><a href="#Music"><span class="tocnumber">7.6</span> <span class="toctext">Music</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-26"><a href="#National_identity"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">National identity</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-27"><a href="#Notes"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">Notes</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_history">Political history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Political history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_Middle_Ages">Early Middle Ages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Early Middle Ages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Scotland_in_the_Early_Middle_Ages" title="Scotland in the Early Middle Ages">Scotland in the Early Middle Ages</a></div>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Minor_kingdoms">Minor kingdoms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Minor kingdoms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Early_Medieval_Scotland_areas.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Early_Medieval_Scotland_areas.png/170px-Early_Medieval_Scotland_areas.png" width="170" height="240" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Early_Medieval_Scotland_areas.png/255px-Early_Medieval_Scotland_areas.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Early_Medieval_Scotland_areas.png/340px-Early_Medieval_Scotland_areas.png 2x" data-file-width="794" data-file-height="1123" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Early_Medieval_Scotland_areas.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Major political centres in early Medieval Scotland</div></div></div>\n<p>In the centuries after the departure of the Romans from Britain, four major circles of influence emerged within the borders of what is now Scotland. In the east were the <a href="/wiki/Picts" title="Picts">Picts</a>, whose kingdoms eventually stretched from the river Forth to Shetland. The first identifiable king to have exerted a superior and wide-ranging authority, was <a href="/wiki/Bridei_I_of_the_Picts" class="mw-redirect" title="Bridei I of the Picts">Bridei mac Maelchon</a> (r. c. 550\xe2\x80\x9384), whose power was based in the Kingdom of Fidach and his base was at the fort of Craig Phadrig near modern <a href="/wiki/Inverness" title="Inverness">Inverness</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Haywoodp2004p116_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Haywoodp2004p116-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> After his death leadership seems to have shifted to the <a href="/wiki/Fortriu" title="Fortriu">Fortriu</a>, whose lands were centred on <a href="/wiki/Strathearn" title="Strathearn">Strathearn</a> and <a href="/wiki/Menteith" title="Menteith">Menteith</a> and who raided along the eastern coast into modern England. Christian missionaries from <a href="/wiki/Iona" title="Iona">Iona</a> appear to have begun the conversion of the Picts to Christianity from 563.<sup id="cite_ref-Smyth1989pp43-6_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smyth1989pp43-6-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the west were the Gaelic (<a href="/wiki/Goidelic" class="mw-redirect" title="Goidelic">Goidelic</a>)-speaking people of <a href="/wiki/D%C3%A1l_Riata" title="D\xc3\xa1l Riata">D\xc3\xa1l Riata</a> with their royal fortress at <a href="/wiki/Dunadd" title="Dunadd">Dunadd</a> in Argyll, with close links with the island of Ireland, from which they brought with them the name Scots. In 563 a mission from Ireland under <a href="/wiki/St._Columba" class="mw-redirect" title="St. Columba">St. Columba</a> founded the monastery of <a href="/wiki/Iona" title="Iona">Iona</a> off the west coast of Scotland and probably began the conversion of the region to Christianity.<sup id="cite_ref-Smyth1989pp43-6_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smyth1989pp43-6-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> The kingdom reached its height under <a href="/wiki/%C3%81ed%C3%A1n_mac_Gabr%C3%A1in" title="\xc3\x81ed\xc3\xa1n mac Gabr\xc3\xa1in">\xc3\x81ed\xc3\xa1n mac Gabr\xc3\xa1in</a> (r. 574\xe2\x80\x93608), but its expansion was checked at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Degsastan" title="Battle of Degsastan">Battle of Degsastan</a> in 603 by <a href="/wiki/%C3%86thelfrith_of_Northumbria" class="mw-redirect" title="\xc3\x86thelfrith of Northumbria">\xc3\x86thelfrith of Northumbria</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the south was the British (<a href="/wiki/Britons_(historical)" class="mw-redirect" title="Britons (historical)">Brythonic</a>) <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Strathclyde" title="Kingdom of Strathclyde">Kingdom of Strathclyde</a>, descendants of the peoples of the Roman influenced kingdoms of "<a href="/wiki/Y_Gogledd_Hen" class="mw-redirect" title="Y Gogledd Hen">The Old North</a>", often named Alt Clut, the Brythonic name for their capital at <a href="/wiki/Dumbarton_Rock" class="mw-redirect" title="Dumbarton Rock">Dumbarton Rock</a>. In 642, they defeated the men of D\xc3\xa1l Riata,<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> but the kingdom suffered a number of attacks from the Picts, and later their Northumbrian allies, between 744 and 756.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> After this, little is recorded until Alt Clut was burnt and probably destroyed in 780, although by whom and what in what circumstances is not known.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Finally, there were the English or "Angles", Germanic invaders who had overrun much of southern Britain and held the Kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Bernicia" title="Bernicia">Bernicia</a>, in the south-east.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> The first English king in the historical record is <a href="/wiki/Ida_of_Bernicia" title="Ida of Bernicia">Ida</a>, who is said to have obtained the throne and the kingdom about 547.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> Ida\'s grandson, \xc3\x86thelfrith, united his kingdom with <a href="/wiki/Deira_(kingdom)" class="mw-redirect" title="Deira (kingdom)">Deira</a> to the south to form Northumbria around the year 604. There were changes of dynasty, and the kingdom was divided, but it was re-united under \xc3\x86thelfrith\'s son <a href="/wiki/Oswald_of_Northumbria" title="Oswald of Northumbria">Oswald</a> (r. 634\xe2\x80\x9342), who had converted to Christianity while in exile in D\xc3\xa1l Riata and looked to Iona for missionaries to help convert his kingdom.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Origins_of_the_Kingdom_of_Alba">Origins of the Kingdom of Alba</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Origins of the Kingdom of Alba">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Origins_of_the_Kingdom_of_Alba" title="Origins of the Kingdom of Alba">Origins of the Kingdom of Alba</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Wikinger.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Wikinger.jpg/170px-Wikinger.jpg" width="170" height="258" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/cb/Wikinger.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="222" data-file-height="337" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Wikinger.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Danish seamen, painted mid-twelfth century</div></div></div>\n<p>This situation was transformed in AD 793 when ferocious Viking raids began on monasteries like Iona and Lindisfarne, creating fear and confusion across the kingdoms of North Britain. <a href="/wiki/Orkney_Islands" class="mw-redirect" title="Orkney Islands">Orkney</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shetland_Isles" class="mw-redirect" title="Shetland Isles">Shetland</a> and the Western Isles eventually fell to the Norsemen.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> The King of Fortriu, <a href="/wiki/Uen_of_the_Picts" class="mw-redirect" title="Uen of the Picts">E\xc3\xb3gan mac \xc3\x93engusa</a>, and the King of D\xc3\xa1l Riata <a href="/wiki/%C3%81ed_mac_Boanta" title="\xc3\x81ed mac Boanta">\xc3\x81ed mac Boanta</a>, were among the dead in a major defeat at the hands of the Vikings in 839.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> A mixture of Viking and Gaelic Irish settlement into south-west Scotland produced the <a href="/wiki/Gall-Gaidel" class="mw-redirect" title="Gall-Gaidel">Gall-Gaidel</a>, the <i>Norse Irish</i>, from which the region gets the modern name <a href="/wiki/Galloway" title="Galloway">Galloway</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> Sometime in the ninth century the beleaguered Kingdom of D\xc3\xa1l Riata lost the Hebrides to the Vikings, when <a href="/wiki/Ketil_Flatnose" class="mw-redirect" title="Ketil Flatnose">Ketil Flatnose</a> is said to have founded the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_the_Isles" title="Kingdom of the Isles">Kingdom of the Isles</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>These threats may have speeded a long term process of gaelicisation of the Pictish kingdoms, which adopted Gaelic language and customs. There was also a merger of the Gaelic and Pictish crowns, although historians debate whether it was a Pictish takeover of D\xc3\xa1l Riata, or the other way around. This culminated in the rise of <a href="/wiki/Kenneth_I_of_Scotland" class="mw-redirect" title="Kenneth I of Scotland">C\xc3\xadnaed mac Ailp\xc3\xadn</a> (Kenneth MacAlpin) in the 840s, which brought to power the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Alpin" title="House of Alpin">House of Alpin</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Yorke2006p54_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yorke2006p54-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> In AD 867 the Vikings seized Northumbria, forming the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_York" class="mw-redirect" title="Kingdom of York">Kingdom of York</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-Rollason2003p212_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rollason2003p212-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> three years later they stormed the Britons\' fortress of Dumbarton<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> and subsequently conquered much of England except for a reduced Kingdom of Wessex,<sup id="cite_ref-Rollason2003p212_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rollason2003p212-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> leaving the new combined Pictish and Gaelic kingdom almost encircled.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> When he died as king of the combined kingdom in 900, <a href="/wiki/Donald_II_of_Scotland" title="Donald II of Scotland">Domnall II</a> (Donald II) was the first man to be called <i>r\xc3\xad Alban</i> (i.e. <i>King of Alba</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> The term Scotia would be increasingly be used to describe the kingdom between North of the Forth and Clyde and eventually the entire area controlled by its kings would be referred to as Scotland.<sup id="cite_ref-Webster1997p22_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Webster1997p22-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="High_Middle_Ages">High Middle Ages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: High Middle Ages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Scotland_in_the_High_Middle_Ages" title="Scotland in the High Middle Ages">Scotland in the High Middle Ages</a></div>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Gaelic_kings:_Constantine_II_to_Alexander_I">Gaelic kings: Constantine II to Alexander I</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Gaelic kings: Constantine II to Alexander I">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/House_of_Dunkeld" title="House of Dunkeld">House of Dunkeld</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Scotland_from_the_Matthew_Paris_map,_c.1250.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Scotland_from_the_Matthew_Paris_map%2C_c.1250.jpg/220px-Scotland_from_the_Matthew_Paris_map%2C_c.1250.jpg" width="220" height="168" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Scotland_from_the_Matthew_Paris_map%2C_c.1250.jpg/330px-Scotland_from_the_Matthew_Paris_map%2C_c.1250.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Scotland_from_the_Matthew_Paris_map%2C_c.1250.jpg/440px-Scotland_from_the_Matthew_Paris_map%2C_c.1250.jpg 2x" data-file-width="522" data-file-height="399" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Scotland_from_the_Matthew_Paris_map,_c.1250.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Scotland from the Matthew Paris map, c. 1250</div></div></div>\n<p>The long reign (900\xe2\x80\x93942/3) of <a href="/wiki/Constantine_II_of_Scotland" title="Constantine II of Scotland">Causant\xc3\xadn</a> (Constantine II) is often regarded as the key to formation of the Kingdom of Alba. He was later credited with bringing Scottish Christianity into conformity with the Catholic Church. After fighting many battles, his defeat at <a href="/wiki/Brunanburh" class="mw-redirect" title="Brunanburh">Brunanburh</a> was followed by his retirement as a <a href="/wiki/Culdee" class="mw-redirect" title="Culdee">Culdee</a> monk at St. Andrews.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> The period between the accession of his successor <a href="/wiki/Malcolm_I_of_Scotland" title="Malcolm I of Scotland">M\xc3\xa1el Coluim I</a> (Malcolm I) and M\xc3\xa1el Coluim mac Cin\xc3\xa1eda (Malcolm II) was marked by good relations with the <a href="/wiki/Wessex" title="Wessex">Wessex</a> <a href="/wiki/Kings_of_England" class="mw-redirect" title="Kings of England">rulers of England</a>, intense internal dynastic disunity and relatively successful expansionary policies. In 945, M\xc3\xa1el Coluim I annexed <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Strathclyde" title="Kingdom of Strathclyde">Strathclyde</a>, where the kings of Alba had probably exercised some authority since the later ninth century, as part of an agreement with King <a href="/wiki/Edmund_I_of_England" class="mw-redirect" title="Edmund I of England">Edmund of England</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> This event was offset by loss of control in Moray. The reign of King <a href="/wiki/Duncan_I_of_Scotland" title="Duncan I of Scotland">Donnchad I</a> (Duncan I) from 1034 was marred by failed military adventures, and he was defeated and killed by <a href="/wiki/Macbeth,_King_of_Scotland" title="Macbeth, King of Scotland">MacBeth</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Mormaer_of_Moray" title="Mormaer of Moray">Mormaer of Moray</a>, who became king in 1040.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> MacBeth ruled for 17 years before he was overthrown by <a href="/wiki/Malcolm_III_of_Scotland" title="Malcolm III of Scotland">M\xc3\xa1el Coluim</a>, the son of Donnchad, who some months later defeated MacBeth\'s <a href="/wiki/Stepfamily" title="Stepfamily">step-son</a> and successor <a href="/wiki/Lulach" title="Lulach">Lulach</a> to become king M\xc3\xa1el Coluim III (Malcolm III).<sup id="cite_ref-Mackie43_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mackie43-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>It was M\xc3\xa1el Coluim III, who acquired the nickname "Canmore" (<i>Cenn M\xc3\xb3r</i>, "Great Chief"), which he passed to his successors and who did most to create the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Dunkeld" title="House of Dunkeld">Dunkeld dynasty</a> that ruled Scotland for the following two centuries. Particularly important was his second marriage to the Anglo-Hungarian princess <a href="/wiki/Saint_Margaret_of_Scotland" title="Saint Margaret of Scotland">Margaret</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> This marriage, and raids on northern England, prompted <a href="/wiki/William_I_of_England" class="mw-redirect" title="William I of England">William the Conqueror</a> to invade and M\xc3\xa1el Coluim submitted to his authority, opening up Scotland to later claims of sovereignty by English kings.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> When Malcolm died in 1093, his brother <a href="/wiki/Donald_III_of_Scotland" title="Donald III of Scotland">Domnall III</a> (Donald III) succeeded him. However, <a href="/wiki/William_II_of_England" title="William II of England">William II of England</a> backed M\xc3\xa1el Coluim\'s son by his first marriage, <a href="/wiki/Duncan_II" class="mw-redirect" title="Duncan II">Donnchad</a>, as a pretender to the throne and he seized power. His murder within a few months saw Domnall restored with one of M\xc3\xa1el Coluim sons by his second marriage, <a href="/wiki/Edmund_of_Scotland" title="Edmund of Scotland">Edmund</a>, as his heir. The two ruled Scotland until two of Edmund\'s younger brothers returned from exile in England, again with English military backing. Victorious, <a href="/wiki/Edgar_of_Scotland" class="mw-redirect" title="Edgar of Scotland">Edgar</a>, the oldest of the three, became king in 1097.<sup id="cite_ref-Webster1997pp23-4_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Webster1997pp23-4-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> Shortly afterwards Edgar and the King of Norway, <a href="/wiki/Magnus_III_of_Norway" class="mw-redirect" title="Magnus III of Norway">Magnus Bare Legs</a> concluded a treaty recognising Norwegian authority over the Western Isles. In practice Norse control of the Isles was loose, with local chiefs enjoying a high degree of independence. He was succeeded by his brother <a href="/wiki/Alexander_I_of_Scotland" title="Alexander I of Scotland">Alexander</a>, who reigned 1107\xe2\x80\x9324.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Scoto-Norman_kings:_David_I_to_Alexander_III">Scoto-Norman kings: David I to Alexander III</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Scoto-Norman kings: David I to Alexander III">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Davidian_Revolution" title="Davidian Revolution">Davidian Revolution</a> and <a href="/wiki/Scoto-Norman" title="Scoto-Norman">Scoto-Norman</a></div><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:David_I_and_Malcolm_IV.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/David_I_and_Malcolm_IV.jpg/220px-David_I_and_Malcolm_IV.jpg" width="220" height="169" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/68/David_I_and_Malcolm_IV.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="330" data-file-height="253" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:David_I_and_Malcolm_IV.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/David_I_of_Scotland" title="David I of Scotland">David I</a> alongside his successor, <a href="/wiki/Malcolm_IV_of_Scotland" title="Malcolm IV of Scotland">Malcolm IV</a></div></div></div>\n<p>When Alexander died in 1124, the crown passed to Margaret\'s fourth son <a href="/wiki/David_I_of_Scotland" title="David I of Scotland">David I</a>, who had spent most of his life as an English baron. His reign saw what has been characterised as a  "<a href="/wiki/Davidian_Revolution" title="Davidian Revolution">Davidian Revolution</a>", by which native institutions and personnel were replaced by English and French ones, underpinning the development of later Medieval Scotland.<sup id="cite_ref-Barrow1992pp9-11_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barrow1992pp9-11-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> Members of the Anglo-Norman nobility took up places in the Scottish aristocracy and he introduced a system of <a href="/wiki/Feudalism" title="Feudalism">feudal</a> land tenure, which produced <a href="/wiki/Knight_service" class="mw-redirect" title="Knight service">knight service</a>, castles and an available body of heavily armed body of cavalry. He created an Anglo-Norman style of court, introduced the office of <a href="/wiki/Justicar" class="mw-redirect" title="Justicar">justicar</a> to oversee justice, and local offices of <a href="/wiki/Sheriffs" class="mw-redirect" title="Sheriffs">sheriffs</a> to administer localities. He established the first <a href="/wiki/Royal_burgh" title="Royal burgh">royal burghs</a> in Scotland, granting rights to particular settlements, which led to the development of the first true Scottish towns and helped facilitate economic development as did the introduction of the first recorded Scottish coinage. He continued a process begun by his mother and brothers, of helping to establish foundations that brought the reformed monasticism based on that at <a href="/wiki/Cluny_Abbey" title="Cluny Abbey">Cluny</a>. He also played a part in the organisation of diocese on lines closer to those in the rest of Western Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-Webster1997pp29-37_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Webster1997pp29-37-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>These reforms were pursued under his successors and grandchildren <a href="/wiki/Malcolm_IV_of_Scotland" title="Malcolm IV of Scotland">Malcolm IV of Scotland</a> and <a href="/wiki/William_I_of_Scotland" class="mw-redirect" title="William I of Scotland">William I</a>, with the crown now passing down the main line of descent through primogeniture, leading to the first of a series of minorities.<sup id="cite_ref-Webster1997pp23-4_26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Webster1997pp23-4-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> The benefits of greater authority were reaped by William\'s son <a href="/wiki/Alexander_II_of_Scotland" title="Alexander II of Scotland">Alexander II</a> and his son <a href="/wiki/Alexander_III_of_Scotland" title="Alexander III of Scotland">Alexander III</a>, who pursued a policy of peace with England to expand their authority in the Highlands and Islands. By the reign of Alexander III, the Scots were in a position to annexe the remainder of the western seaboard, which they did following <a href="/wiki/Haakon_IV_of_Norway" title="Haakon IV of Norway">Haakon Haakonarson</a>\'s ill-fated invasion and the stalemate of the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Largs" title="Battle of Largs">Battle of Largs</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Perth" title="Treaty of Perth">Treaty of Perth</a> in 1266.<sup id="cite_ref-Macquarrie2004p153_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Macquarrie2004p153-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Late_Middle_Ages">Late Middle Ages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Late Middle Ages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Scotland_in_the_Late_Middle_Ages" title="Scotland in the Late Middle Ages">Scotland in the Late Middle Ages</a></div>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Wars_of_Independence:_Margaret_to_David_II">Wars of Independence: Margaret to David II</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Wars of Independence: Margaret to David II">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Wars_of_Scottish_Independence" title="Wars of Scottish Independence">Wars of Scottish Independence</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Robert_the_Bruce.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Robert_the_Bruce.jpg/170px-Robert_the_Bruce.jpg" width="170" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Robert_the_Bruce.jpg/255px-Robert_the_Bruce.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Robert_the_Bruce.jpg/340px-Robert_the_Bruce.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1772" data-file-height="2362" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Robert_the_Bruce.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The statue near Stirling commemorating <a href="/wiki/Robert_I_of_Scotland" class="mw-redirect" title="Robert I of Scotland">Robert I</a></div></div></div>\n<p>The death of King Alexander III in 1286, and then of his granddaughter and heir <a href="/wiki/Margaret,_Maid_of_Norway" title="Margaret, Maid of Norway">Margaret, Maid of Norway</a> in 1290, left 14 rivals for succession. To prevent civil war the Scottish magnates asked <a href="/wiki/Edward_I_of_England" title="Edward I of England">Edward I of England</a> to arbitrate, for which he extracted legal recognition that the realm of Scotland was held as a feudal dependency to the throne of England before choosing <a href="/wiki/John_Balliol" title="John Balliol">John Balliol</a>, the man with the strongest claim, who became king in 1292.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Robert_Bruce,_5th_Lord_of_Annandale" class="mw-redirect" title="Robert Bruce, 5th Lord of Annandale">Robert Bruce, 5th Lord of Annandale</a>, the next strongest claimant, accepted this outcome with reluctance. Over the next few years Edward I used the concessions he had gained to systematically undermine both the authority of King John and the independence of Scotland.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> In 1295 John, on the urgings of his chief councillors, entered into an alliance with France, known as the <a href="/wiki/Auld_Alliance" title="Auld Alliance">Auld Alliance</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> In 1296 Edward invaded Scotland, deposing King John. The following year <a href="/wiki/William_Wallace" title="William Wallace">William Wallace</a> and <a href="/wiki/Andrew_de_Moray" class="mw-redirect" title="Andrew de Moray">Andrew de Moray</a> raised forces to resist the occupation and under their joint leadership an English army was defeated at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Stirling_Bridge" title="Battle of Stirling Bridge">Battle of Stirling Bridge</a>. For a short time Wallace ruled Scotland in the name of John Balliol as guardian of the realm. Edward came north in person and defeated Wallace at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Falkirk_(1298)" class="mw-redirect" title="Battle of Falkirk (1298)">Battle of Falkirk</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> The English barons refuted the French-inspired papal claim to Scottish overlordship in the <a href="/wiki/Barons%27_Letter,_1301" class="mw-redirect" title="Barons&#39; Letter, 1301">Barons\' Letter, 1301</a>, claiming it rather as long possessed by English kings. Wallace escaped but probably resigned as Guardian of Scotland. In 1305 he fell into the hands of the English, who executed him for treason despite the fact that he believed he owed no allegiance to England.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Rivals <a href="/wiki/John_III_Comyn,_Lord_of_Badenoch" title="John III Comyn, Lord of Badenoch">John Comyn</a> and <a href="/wiki/Robert_the_Bruce" title="Robert the Bruce">Robert the Bruce</a>, grandson of the claimant, were appointed as joint guardians in his place.<sup id="cite_ref-Ross1999_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ross1999-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> On 10 February 1306, Bruce participated in the murder of Comyn, at Greyfriars Kirk in <a href="/wiki/Dumfries" title="Dumfries">Dumfries</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> Less than seven weeks later, on 25 March, Bruce was crowned as king. However, Edward\'s forces overran the country after defeating Bruce\'s small army at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Methven" title="Battle of Methven">Battle of Methven</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> Despite the excommunication of Bruce and his followers by <a href="/wiki/Pope_Clement_V" title="Pope Clement V">Pope Clement V</a>, his support slowly strengthened; and by 1314 with the help of leading nobles such as Sir <a href="/wiki/James_Douglas,_Lord_of_Douglas" title="James Douglas, Lord of Douglas">James Douglas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Randolph,_1st_Earl_of_Moray" title="Thomas Randolph, 1st Earl of Moray">Thomas Randolph</a> only the castles at Bothwell and Stirling remained under English control.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> Edward I had died in 1307. His heir <a href="/wiki/Edward_II_of_England" title="Edward II of England">Edward II</a> moved an army north to break the siege of <a href="/wiki/Stirling_Castle" title="Stirling Castle">Stirling Castle</a> and reassert control. Robert defeated that army at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Bannockburn" title="Battle of Bannockburn">Battle of Bannockburn</a> in 1314, securing <i>de facto</i> independence.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> In 1320 the <a href="/wiki/Declaration_of_Arbroath" title="Declaration of Arbroath">Declaration of Arbroath</a>, a remonstrance to the pope from the nobles of Scotland, helped convince <a href="/wiki/Pope_John_XXII" title="Pope John XXII">Pope John XXII</a> to overturn the earlier excommunication and nullify the various acts of submission by Scottish kings to English ones so that Scotland\'s sovereignty could be recognised by the major European dynasties. The declaration has also been seen as one of the most important documents in the development of a Scottish national identity.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>\nIn 1328, <a href="/wiki/Edward_III_of_England" title="Edward III of England">Edward III</a> signed the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Northampton" class="mw-redirect" title="Treaty of Northampton">Treaty of Northampton</a> acknowledging <a href="/wiki/Scottish_independence" title="Scottish independence">Scottish independence</a> under the rule of Robert the Bruce.<sup id="cite_ref-Keen2003_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Keen2003-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> However, four years after Robert\'s death in 1329, England once more invaded on the pretext of restoring <a href="/wiki/Edward_Balliol" title="Edward Balliol">Edward Balliol</a>, son of John Balliol, to the Scottish throne, thus starting the Second War of Independence.<sup id="cite_ref-Keen2003_43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Keen2003-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> Despite victories at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Dupplin_Moor" title="Battle of Dupplin Moor">Dupplin Moor</a> and <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Halidon_Hill" title="Battle of Halidon Hill">Halidon Hill</a>, in the face of tough Scottish resistance led by <a href="/wiki/Sir_Andrew_Murray" class="mw-redirect" title="Sir Andrew Murray">Sir Andrew Murray</a>, the son of Wallace\'s comrade in arms, successive attempts to secure Balliol on the throne failed.<sup id="cite_ref-Keen2003_43-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Keen2003-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> Edward III lost interest in the fate of his protege after the outbreak of the <a href="/wiki/Hundred_Years%27_War" title="Hundred Years&#39; War">Hundred Years\' War</a> with France.<sup id="cite_ref-Keen2003_43-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Keen2003-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> In 1341 <a href="/wiki/David_II_of_Scotland" title="David II of Scotland">David II</a>, King Robert\'s son and heir, was able to return from temporary exile in France. Balliol finally resigned his claim to the throne to Edward in 1356, before retiring to Yorkshire, where he died in 1364.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup></p><div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Stewarts:_Robert_II_to_James_IV">The Stewarts: Robert II to James IV</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: The Stewarts: Robert II to James IV">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/House_of_Stuart" title="House of Stuart">House of Stuart</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:James_II_of_Scotland_(by_von_Ehingen).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/James_II_of_Scotland_%28by_von_Ehingen%29.jpg/170px-James_II_of_Scotland_%28by_von_Ehingen%29.jpg" width="170" height="239" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/James_II_of_Scotland_%28by_von_Ehingen%29.jpg/255px-James_II_of_Scotland_%28by_von_Ehingen%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/a7/James_II_of_Scotland_%28by_von_Ehingen%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="305" data-file-height="429" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:James_II_of_Scotland_(by_von_Ehingen).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/James_II_of_Scotland" title="James II of Scotland">James II</a> (r. 1437\xe2\x80\x9360), one of the most successful members of the <a href="/wiki/Stewart_dynasty" class="mw-redirect" title="Stewart dynasty">Stewart dynasty</a> before his accidental death at the siege of Roxburgh</div></div></div>\n<p>After David II\'s death, <a href="/wiki/Robert_II_of_Scotland" title="Robert II of Scotland">Robert II</a>, the first of the Stewart kings, came to the throne in 1371. He was followed in 1390 by his ailing son John, who took the <a href="/wiki/Regnal_name" title="Regnal name">regnal name</a> <a href="/wiki/Robert_III_of_Scotland" title="Robert III of Scotland">Robert III</a>. During Robert III\'s reign (1390\xe2\x80\x931406), actual power rested largely in the hands of his brother, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Stewart,_Duke_of_Albany" title="Robert Stewart, Duke of Albany">Robert Stewart, Duke of Albany</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Rigby2003_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rigby2003-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> After the suspicious death (possibly on the orders of the Duke of Albany) of his elder son, David, Duke of Rothesay in 1402, Robert, fearful for the safety of his younger son, the future <a href="/wiki/James_I_of_Scotland" title="James I of Scotland">James I</a>, sent him to France in 1406. However, the English captured him en route and he spent the next 18 years as a prisoner held for ransom. As a result, after the death of Robert III, regents ruled Scotland: first, the Duke of Albany; and later his son <a href="/wiki/Murdoch_Stewart,_Duke_of_Albany" title="Murdoch Stewart, Duke of Albany">Murdoch</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>When Scotland finally paid the ransom in 1424, James, aged 32, returned with his English bride determined to assert this authority.<sup id="cite_ref-Rigby2003_45-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rigby2003-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> Several members of the Albany family were executed, and he succeeded in centralising control in the hands of the crown, but at the cost of increasingly unpopularity and he was assassinated in 1437. His son <a href="/wiki/James_II_of_Scotland" title="James II of Scotland">James II</a> when he came of age in 1449, continued his father\'s policy of weakening the great noble families, most notably taking on the powerful <a href="/wiki/House_of_Douglas" class="mw-redirect" title="House of Douglas">Black Douglas family</a> that had come to prominence at the time of Robert I.<sup id="cite_ref-Rigby2003_45-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rigby2003-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> His attempt to take Roxburgh from the English in 1460 succeeded, but at the cost of his life as he was killed by an exploding artillery piece.<sup id="cite_ref-Barrellp160-9_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barrellp160-9-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>His young son came to the throne as <a href="/wiki/James_III_of_Scotland" title="James III of Scotland">James III</a>, resulting in another minority, with <a href="/wiki/Robert_Boyd,_1st_Lord_Boyd" title="Robert Boyd, 1st Lord Boyd">Robert, Lord Boyd</a> emerging as the most important figure. In 1468 James married <a href="/wiki/Margaret_of_Denmark,_Queen_of_Scotland" title="Margaret of Denmark, Queen of Scotland">Margaret of Denmark</a>, receiving the Orkney and the Shetland Islands in payment of her dowry.<sup id="cite_ref-J._Wormald,_1991_p._5_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-J._Wormald,_1991_p._5-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> In 1469 the King asserted his control, executing members of the Boyd family and his brothers, <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Stewart,_1st_Duke_of_Albany" class="mw-redirect" title="Alexander Stewart, 1st Duke of Albany">Alexander, Duke of Albany</a> and <a href="/wiki/John_Stewart,_Earl_of_Mar_(d._1479)" title="John Stewart, Earl of Mar (d. 1479)">John, Earl of Mar</a>, resulting in Albany leading an English backed invasion and becoming effective ruler.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> The English retreated, having taken Berwick for the last time in 1482, and James was able to regain power. However, the King managed to alienate the barons, former supporters, his wife and his son James. He was defeated at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Sauchieburn" title="Battle of Sauchieburn">Battle of Sauchieburn</a> and killed in 1488.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>His successor <a href="/wiki/James_IV_of_Scotland" title="James IV of Scotland">James IV</a> successfully ended the quasi-independent rule of the <a href="/wiki/Lord_of_the_Isles" title="Lord of the Isles">Lord of the Isles</a>, bringing the Western Isles under effective Royal control for the first time.<sup id="cite_ref-Rigby2003_45-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rigby2003-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> In 1503, he married <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Tudor" title="Margaret Tudor">Margaret Tudor</a>, daughter of <a href="/wiki/Henry_VII_of_England" title="Henry VII of England">Henry VII of England</a>, thus laying the foundation for the seventeenth century <a href="/wiki/Union_of_the_Crowns" title="Union of the Crowns">Union of the Crowns</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> However, in 1512 the Auld Alliance was renewed and under its terms, when the French were attacked by the English under <a href="/wiki/Henry_VIII_of_England" title="Henry VIII of England">Henry VIII</a> the next year, James IV invaded England in support. The invasion was stopped decisively at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Flodden" title="Battle of Flodden">Battle of Flodden</a> during which the King, many of his nobles, and a large number of ordinary troops were killed. Once again Scotland\'s government lay in the hands of regents in the name of the infant <a href="/wiki/James_V_of_Scotland" title="James V of Scotland">James V</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-G._Menzies_2002_p._179_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-G._Menzies_2002_p._179-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Government">Government</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Government">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Government_in_Medieval_Scotland" class="mw-redirect" title="Government in Medieval Scotland">Government in Medieval Scotland</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Alexander_III_and_Ollamh_R%C3%ADgh.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Alexander_III_and_Ollamh_R%C3%ADgh.JPG/220px-Alexander_III_and_Ollamh_R%C3%ADgh.JPG" width="220" height="193" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Alexander_III_and_Ollamh_R%C3%ADgh.JPG/330px-Alexander_III_and_Ollamh_R%C3%ADgh.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Alexander_III_and_Ollamh_R%C3%ADgh.JPG/440px-Alexander_III_and_Ollamh_R%C3%ADgh.JPG 2x" data-file-width="887" data-file-height="779" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Alexander_III_and_Ollamh_R%C3%ADgh.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Coronation of <a href="/wiki/Alexander_III_of_Scotland" title="Alexander III of Scotland">Alexander III</a> on <a href="/wiki/Moot_Hill" class="mw-redirect" title="Moot Hill">Moot Hill</a>, <a href="/wiki/Scone,_Perth_and_Kinross" class="mw-redirect" title="Scone, Perth and Kinross">Scone</a>, beside him are the <a href="/wiki/Mormaer_of_Strathearn" class="mw-redirect" title="Mormaer of Strathearn">Mormaers of Strathearn</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mormaer_of_Fife" class="mw-redirect" title="Mormaer of Fife">Fife</a>, while his genealogy is recited by a royal poet</div></div></div>\n<p>Kingship was the major form of political organisation in the Early Middle Ages, with competing minor kingdoms and fluid relationships of over and under kingdoms.<sup id="cite_ref-Yorke2009_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yorke2009-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> The primary function of these kings was as war leaders, but there were also ritual elements to kingship, evident in ceremonies of coronation.The unification of the Scots and Picts from the tenth century that produced the Kingdom of Alba, retained some of these ritual aspects in the coronation at <a href="/wiki/Scone,_Scotland" title="Scone, Scotland">Scone</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Webster1997pp45-7_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Webster1997pp45-7-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> While the Scottish monarchy remained a largely itinerant institution, Scone remained one of its most important locations,<sup id="cite_ref-McNeil&amp;MacQueenpp159-63_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McNeil&amp;MacQueenpp159-63-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> with Royal castles at <a href="/wiki/Stirling_Castle" title="Stirling Castle">Stirling</a> and <a href="/wiki/Perth_Castle" title="Perth Castle">Perth</a> becoming significant in the later Middle Ages before <a href="/wiki/Edinburgh" title="Edinburgh">Edinburgh</a> developed as a capital in the second half of the fifteenth century.<sup id="cite_ref-Wormald1991pp14-5_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wormald1991pp14-5-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> The Scottish crown grew in prestige throughout the era and adopted the conventional offices of Western European courts<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> and later elements of their ritual and grandeur.<sup id="cite_ref-Wormald1991pp14-5_56-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wormald1991pp14-5-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>\nIn the early period the kings of the Scots depended on the great lords of the <a href="/wiki/Mormaer" title="Mormaer">mormaers</a> (later <a href="/wiki/Earl" title="Earl">earls</a>) and To\xc3\xadsechs (later <a href="/wiki/Thanes" class="mw-redirect" title="Thanes">thanes</a>), but from the reign of David I <a href="/wiki/Sheriffdom" title="Sheriffdom">sheriffdoms</a> were introduced, which allowed more direct control and gradually limited the power of the major lordships.<sup id="cite_ref-McNeil&amp;MacQueenpp191-4_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McNeil&amp;MacQueenpp191-4-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> While knowledge of early systems of law is limited, justice can be seen as developing from the twelfth century onwards with local <a href="/wiki/Sheriff_Court" class="mw-redirect" title="Sheriff Court">sheriff</a>, <a href="/wiki/Burgh" title="Burgh">burgh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Manorial_court" title="Manorial court">manorial</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ecclesiastical_court" title="Ecclesiastical court">ecclesiastical courts</a> and offices of the justicar to oversee administration.<sup id="cite_ref-ReidandZimmerman2000Ip24_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReidandZimmerman2000Ip24-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> The Scots <a href="/wiki/Common_law" title="Common law">common law</a> began to develop in this period<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> and there were attempts to systematise and codify the law and the beginnings of an educated professional body of lawyers.<sup id="cite_ref-ReidandZimmerman2000Ip66_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReidandZimmerman2000Ip66-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> In the Late Middle Ages major institutions of government, including the <a href="/wiki/Privy_Council_of_Scotland" title="Privy Council of Scotland">privy council</a> and <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_Scotland" title="Parliament of Scotland">parliament</a> developed. The council emerged as a full-time body in the fifteenth century, increasingly dominated by laymen and critical to the administration of justice. Parliament also emerged as a major legal institution, gaining an oversight of taxation and policy. By the end of the era it was sitting almost every year, partly because of the frequent minorities and regencies of the period, which may have prevented it from being sidelined by the monarchy.<sup id="cite_ref-Wormald1991pp21-3_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wormald1991pp21-3-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup></p><div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Warfare">Warfare</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Warfare">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Warfare_in_Medieval_Scotland" title="Warfare in Medieval Scotland">Warfare in Medieval Scotland</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Otterburn_Battle.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Otterburn_Battle.jpg/220px-Otterburn_Battle.jpg" width="220" height="223" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Otterburn_Battle.jpg/330px-Otterburn_Battle.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Otterburn_Battle.jpg/440px-Otterburn_Battle.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1926" data-file-height="1950" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Otterburn_Battle.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/The_Battle_of_Otterburn" class="mw-redirect" title="The Battle of Otterburn">The Battle of Otterburn</a> (1388) in a miniature from <a href="/wiki/Jean_Froissart" title="Jean Froissart">Jean Froissart</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Froissart%27s_Chronicles" title="Froissart&#39;s Chronicles">Chroniques</a></i></div></div></div>\n<p>In the Early Middle Ages, war on land was characterised by the use of small war-bands of household troops often engaging in raids and low level warfare.<sup id="cite_ref-Alcock_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Alcock-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> The arrival of the Vikings brought a new scale of naval warfare, with rapid movement based around the Viking longship. The <a href="/wiki/Birlinn" title="Birlinn">birlinn</a>, which developed from the longship, became a major factor in warfare in the Highlands and Islands.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> By the <a href="/wiki/Scotland_in_the_High_Middle_Ages" title="Scotland in the High Middle Ages">High Middle Ages</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Kings_of_Scotland" class="mw-redirect" title="Kings of Scotland">kings of Scotland</a> could command forces of tens of thousands of men for short periods as part of the "common army", mainly of poorly armoured spear and bowmen.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> After the introduction of feudalism to Scotland, these forces were augmented by small numbers of mounted and heavily armoured knights.<sup id="cite_ref-Barrow1992pp9-11_28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barrow1992pp9-11-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> Feudalism also introduced castles into the country, originally simple wooden <a href="/wiki/Motte-and-bailey" class="mw-redirect" title="Motte-and-bailey">motte-and-bailey</a> constructions, but these were replaced in the thirteenth century with more formidable stone "<a href="/wiki/Enceinte" title="Enceinte">enceinte</a>" castles, with high encircling walls.<sup id="cite_ref-West1985p26_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-West1985p26-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> In the thirteenth century the threat of Scandinavian naval power subsided and the kings of Scotland were able to use naval forces to help subdue the Highlands and Islands.<sup id="cite_ref-Macquarrie2004p153_31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Macquarrie2004p153-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Scottish field armies rarely managed to stand up to the usually larger and more professional armies produced by England, but they were used to good effect by Robert I at Bannockburn in 1314 to secure Scottish independence.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> He also made use of naval power to support his forces and began to develop a royal Scottish naval force.<sup id="cite_ref-Grant_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Grant-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> Under the Stewart kings these forces were further augmented by specialist troops, particularly <a href="/wiki/Men-at-arms" class="mw-redirect" title="Men-at-arms">men-at-arms</a> and <a href="/wiki/Archery" title="Archery">archers</a>, hired by bonds of <i><a href="/wiki/Manrent" title="Manrent">manrent</a></i>, similar to English <a href="/wiki/Indentures" class="mw-redirect" title="Indentures">indentures</a> of the same period.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown2004_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown2004-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> New "<a href="/wiki/Livery_and_maintenance" class="mw-redirect" title="Livery and maintenance">livery and maintenance</a>" castles were built to house these troops<sup id="cite_ref-West1985p26_66-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-West1985p26-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> and castles began to be adapted to accommodate gunpowder weapons.<sup id="cite_ref-West1985p27_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-West1985p27-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> The Stewarts also adopted major innovations in continental warfare, such as longer pikes <sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> and the extensive use of artillery,<sup id="cite_ref-Caldwell2007_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Caldwell2007-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> and they built up a formidable navy.<sup id="cite_ref-Grant_68-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Grant-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> However, in the early fifteenth century one of the best armed and largest Scottish armies ever assembled still met with defeat at the hands of an English army at the Battle of Flodden in 1513, which saw the destruction of a large number of ordinary troops, a large section of the nobility and the king, James IV.<sup id="cite_ref-G._Menzies_2002_p._179_52-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-G._Menzies_2002_p._179-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Religion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Christianity_in_Medieval_Scotland" title="Christianity in Medieval Scotland">Christianity in Medieval Scotland</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Saint_Margaret_of_Scotland.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Saint_Margaret_of_Scotland.jpg/220px-Saint_Margaret_of_Scotland.jpg" width="220" height="207" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/3d/Saint_Margaret_of_Scotland.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="268" data-file-height="252" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Saint_Margaret_of_Scotland.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/St_Margaret_of_Scotland" class="mw-redirect" title="St Margaret of Scotland">St Margaret of Scotland</a>, credited with the reform of Scottish monasticism, from a later genealogy</div></div></div>\n<p>Christianity was probably introduced to what is now lowland Scotland from Roman soldiers stationed in the north of the province of <a href="/wiki/Roman_Britain" title="Roman Britain">Britannia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> It is presumed to have survived among the Brythonic enclaves in the south of modern Scotland, but retreated as the pagan Anglo-Saxons advanced.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> Scotland was largely converted by Irish-Scots missions associated with figures such as <a href="/wiki/St_Columba" class="mw-redirect" title="St Columba">St Columba</a> from the fifth to the seventh centuries. These missions tended to found <a href="/wiki/Monastery" title="Monastery">monastic</a> institutions and collegiate churches that served large areas.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> Partly as a result of these factors, some scholars have identified a distinctive form of <a href="/wiki/Celtic_Christianity" title="Celtic Christianity">Celtic Christianity</a>, in which <a href="/wiki/Abbot" title="Abbot">abbots</a> were more significant than bishops, attitudes to <a href="/wiki/Clerical_celibacy" title="Clerical celibacy">clerical celibacy</a> were more relaxed and there was some significant differences in practice with Roman Christianity, particularly the form of <a href="/wiki/Tonsure" title="Tonsure">tonsure</a> and the method of <a href="/wiki/Computus" title="Computus">calculating Easter</a>, although most of these issues had been resolved by the mid-seventh century.<sup id="cite_ref-Evans1985_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Evans1985-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>  After the reconversion of <a href="/wiki/Scandinavian_Scotland" title="Scandinavian Scotland">Scandinavian Scotland</a> from the tenth century, Christianity under papal authority was the dominant religion of the kingdom.<sup id="cite_ref-Macquarrie2004pp67-8_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Macquarrie2004pp67-8-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the Norman period the Scottish church underwent a series of reforms and transformations. With royal and lay patronage, a clearer parochial structure based around local churches was developed.<sup id="cite_ref-Macquarrie2004pp109-117_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Macquarrie2004pp109-117-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> Large numbers of new foundations, which followed continental forms of reformed monasticism, began to predominate and the Scottish church established its independence from England, developed a clearer diocesan structure, becoming a "special daughter of the see of Rome", but lacking leadership in the form of archbishops.<sup id="cite_ref-Bawcutt&amp;Williams2006pp26-9_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bawcutt&amp;Williams2006pp26-9-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> In the Late Middle Ages the problems of schism in the Catholic Church allowed the Scottish Crown to gain greater influence over senior appointments and two archbishoprics had been established by the end of the fifteenth century.<sup id="cite_ref-Wormald1991pp75-87_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wormald1991pp75-87-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> While some historians have discerned a decline of monasticism in the Late Middle Ages, the <a href="/wiki/Mendicant" title="Mendicant">mendicant</a> orders of <a href="/wiki/Friar" title="Friar">friars</a> grew, particularly in the expanding <a href="/wiki/Burgh" title="Burgh">burghs</a>, to meet the spiritual needs of the population. New saints and cults of devotion also proliferated. Despite problems over the number and quality of clergy after the <a href="/wiki/Black_Death" title="Black Death">Black Death</a> in the fourteenth century, and some evidence of heresy in this period, the church in Scotland remained relatively stable before the Reformation in the sixteenth century.<sup id="cite_ref-Wormald1991pp75-87_81-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wormald1991pp75-87-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">Geography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Geography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_Scotland_in_the_Middle_Ages" title="Geography of Scotland in the Middle Ages">Geography of Scotland in the Middle Ages</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Anglo-Scottish.border.history.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Anglo-Scottish.border.history.jpg/170px-Anglo-Scottish.border.history.jpg" width="170" height="226" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Anglo-Scottish.border.history.jpg/255px-Anglo-Scottish.border.history.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Anglo-Scottish.border.history.jpg/340px-Anglo-Scottish.border.history.jpg 2x" data-file-width="901" data-file-height="1200" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Anglo-Scottish.border.history.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The development of the border with England</div></div></div>\n<p>Modern Scotland is half the size England and Wales in area, but with its many inlets, islands and inland <a href="/wiki/Lochs" class="mw-redirect" title="Lochs">lochs</a>, it has roughly the same amount of coastline at 4,000 miles. Only a fifth of Scotland is less than 60 metres above sea level. Its east Atlantic position means that it has very heavy rainfall: today about 700&#160;cm per year in the east and over 1,000&#160;cm in the west. This encouraged the spread of blanket <a href="/wiki/Peat_bog" class="mw-redirect" title="Peat bog">peat bog</a>, the acidity of which, combined with high level of wind and salt spray, made most of the islands treeless. The existence of hills, mountains, quicksands and marshes made internal communication and conquest extremely difficult and may have contributed to the fragmented nature of political power.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The defining factor in the geography of Scotland is the distinction between the <a href="/wiki/Highlands_and_Islands" title="Highlands and Islands">Highlands and Islands</a> in the north and west and the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_lowlands" class="mw-redirect" title="Scottish lowlands">Lowlands</a> in the south and east. The highlands are further divided into the <a href="/wiki/Northwest_Highlands" title="Northwest Highlands">Northwest Highlands</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Grampian_Mountains" title="Grampian Mountains">Grampian Mountains</a> by the fault line of the <a href="/wiki/Great_Glen" title="Great Glen">Great Glen</a>. The lowlands are divided into the fertile belt of the <a href="/wiki/Central_Lowlands" title="Central Lowlands">Central Lowlands</a> and the higher terrain of the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Uplands" title="Southern Uplands">Southern Uplands</a>, which included the <a href="/wiki/Cheviot_hills" class="mw-redirect" title="Cheviot hills">Cheviot hills</a>, over which the border with England came to run by the end of the period.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> Some of these were further divided by mountains, major rivers and marshes.<sup id="cite_ref-Webster1997pp9-20_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Webster1997pp9-20-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> The Central Lowland belt averages about 50 miles in width<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> and, because it contains most of the good quality agricultural land and has easier communications, could support most of the urbanisation and elements of conventional Medieval government.<sup id="cite_ref-Wormald1991pp39-40_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wormald1991pp39-40-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> However, the Southern Uplands, and particularly the Highlands were economically less productive and much more difficult to govern. This provided Scotland with a form of protection, as minor English incursions had to cross the difficult southern uplands<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> and the two major attempts at conquest by the English, under Edward I and then Edward III, were unable to penetrate the highlands, from which area potential resistance could reconquer the Lowlands.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> However, it also made those areas problematic to govern for Scottish kings and much of the political history of the era after the wars of independence circulated around attempts to resolve problems of entrenched localism in these regions.<sup id="cite_ref-Wormald1991pp39-40_86-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wormald1991pp39-40-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Until the thirteenth century the borders with England were very fluid, with Northumbria being annexed to Scotland by David I, but lost under his grandson and successor Malcolm IV in 1157.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> By the late thirteenth century when the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_York" title="Treaty of York">Treaty of York</a> (1237) and <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Perth" title="Treaty of Perth">Treaty of Perth</a> (1266) had fixed the boundaries with the Kingdom of the Scots with England and <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a> respectively, its borders were close to the modern boundaries.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Man" title="Isle of Man">Isle of Man</a> fell under English control in the fourteenth century, despite several attempts to restore Scottish authority.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> The English were able to annexe a large slice of the Lowlands under Edward III, but these losses were gradually regained, particularly while England was preoccupied with the <a href="/wiki/Wars_of_the_Roses" title="Wars of the Roses">Wars of the Roses</a> (1455\xe2\x80\x9385).<sup id="cite_ref-Bawcutt&amp;Williams2006p21_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bawcutt&amp;Williams2006p21-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> The dowry of the Orkney and Shetland Islands in 1468 was the last great land acquisition for the kingdom.<sup id="cite_ref-J._Wormald,_1991_p._5_48-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-J._Wormald,_1991_p._5-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> However, in 1482 Berwick, a border fortress and the largest port in Medieval Scotland, fell to the English once again, for what was to be the final change of hands.<sup id="cite_ref-Bawcutt&amp;Williams2006p21_92-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bawcutt&amp;Williams2006p21-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economy_and_society">Economy and society</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Economy and society">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Economy">Economy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Economy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_Scotland_in_the_Middle_Ages" title="Economy of Scotland in the Middle Ages">Economy of Scotland in the Middle Ages</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Dab%C3%ADd_mac_Ma%C3%ADl_Choluim_Coinage.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Dab%C3%ADd_mac_Ma%C3%ADl_Choluim_Coinage.JPG/170px-Dab%C3%ADd_mac_Ma%C3%ADl_Choluim_Coinage.JPG" width="170" height="296" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/25/Dab%C3%ADd_mac_Ma%C3%ADl_Choluim_Coinage.JPG 1.5x" data-file-width="221" data-file-height="385" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Dab%C3%ADd_mac_Ma%C3%ADl_Choluim_Coinage.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A silver penny of <a href="/wiki/David_I_of_Scotland" title="David I of Scotland">David I</a>, the first silver coinage to bear a Scottish king\'s head</div></div></div>\n<p>Having between a fifth or sixth of the arable or good pastoral land and roughly the same amount of coastline as England and Wales, marginal pastoral agriculture and fishing were two of the most important aspects of the Medieval Scottish economy.<sup id="cite_ref-Gemmill&amp;Mayhew1995pp8-10_93-0"
