class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gemmill&amp;Mayhew1995pp8-10-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> With poor communications, in the Early Middle Ages most settlements needed to achieve a degree of self-sufficiency in agriculture.<sup id="cite_ref-Woolf2007pp17-20_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Woolf2007pp17-20-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> Most farms were based around a family unit and used an infield and outfield system.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> Arable farming grew in the High Middle Ages<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> and agriculture entered a period of relative boom between the thirteenth century and late fifteenth century.<sup id="cite_ref-Rigby2003pp111-16_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rigby2003pp111-16-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Unlike England, Scotland had no towns dating from Roman occupation. From the twelfth century there are records of burghs, chartered towns, which became major centres of crafts and trade.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> and there is evidence of 55 burghs by 1296.<sup id="cite_ref-Webster1997pp122-3_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Webster1997pp122-3-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> There are also Scottish coins, although English coinage probably remained more significant in trade and until the end of the period barter was probably the most common form of exchange.<sup id="cite_ref-MacQuarrie2004pp136-40_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MacQuarrie2004pp136-40-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Stringer2005_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stringer2005-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> Nevertheless, craft and industry remained relatively undeveloped before the end of the Middle Ages<sup id="cite_ref-Wormald1991pp41-55_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wormald1991pp41-55-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> and, although there were extensive trading networks based in Scotland, while the Scots exported largely raw materials, they imported increasing quantities of luxury goods, resulting in a bullion shortage and perhaps helping to create a financial crisis in the fifteenth century.<sup id="cite_ref-Wormald1991pp41-55_102-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wormald1991pp41-55-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Demography">Demography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Demography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Demography_of_Scotland" title="Demography of Scotland">Demography of Scotland</a></div>\n<p>There are almost no written sources from which to re-construct the demography of early Medieval Scotland. Estimates have been made of a population of 10,000 inhabitants in D\xc3\xa1l Riata and 80\xe2\x80\x93100,000 for Pictland.<sup id="cite_ref-Lang2006pp21-2_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lang2006pp21-2-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> It is likely that the 5th and 6th centuries saw higher mortality rates due to the appearance of <a href="/wiki/Bubonic_plague" title="Bubonic plague">bubonic plague</a>, which may have reduced net population.<sup id="cite_ref-Fouracre&amp;McKitterick2005p.234_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fouracre&amp;McKitterick2005p.234-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> The examination of burial sites for this period like that at Hallowhill, <a href="/wiki/St_Andrews" title="St Andrews">St Andrews</a> indicate a life expectancy of only 26-9.<sup id="cite_ref-Lang2006pp21-2_103-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lang2006pp21-2-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> The known conditions have been taken to suggest it was a high fertility, high mortality society, similar to many developing countries in the modern world, with a relatively young demographic profile, and perhaps early childbearing, and large numbers of children for women. This would have meant that there were a relatively small proportion of available workers to the number of mouths to feed. This have made it difficult to produce a surplus that would allow demographic growth and more complex societies to develop.<sup id="cite_ref-Woolf2007pp17-20_94-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Woolf2007pp17-20-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> From the formation of the Kingdom of Alba in the tenth century, to before the <a href="/wiki/Black_Death" title="Black Death">Black Death</a> reached the country in 1349, estimates based on the amount of farmable land, suggest that population may have grown from half a million to a million.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> Although there is no reliable documentation on the impact of the plague, there are many anecdotal references to abandoned land in the following decades. If the pattern followed that in England, then the population may have fallen to as low as half a million by the end of the fifteenth century.<sup id="cite_ref-Rigby2003p109-11_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rigby2003p109-11-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> Compared with the situation after the redistribution of population in the later <a href="/wiki/Highland_clearances" class="mw-redirect" title="Highland clearances">clearances</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_revolution" class="mw-redirect" title="Industrial revolution">industrial revolution</a>, these numbers would have been relatively evenly spread over the kingdom, with roughly half living north of the Tay.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> Perhaps ten per cent of the population lived in one of many burghs that grew up in the later Medieval period, mainly in the east and south. It has been suggested that they would have had a mean population of about 2,000, but many would be much smaller than 1,000 and the largest, Edinburgh, probably had a population of over 10,000 by the end of the era.<sup id="cite_ref-Gemmill&amp;Mayhew1995pp8-10_93-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gemmill&amp;Mayhew1995pp8-10-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Social_structure">Social structure</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Social structure">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Scottish_society_in_the_Middle_Ages" title="Scottish society in the Middle Ages">Scottish society in the Middle Ages</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Scottish_clan_map.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Scottish_clan_map.png/170px-Scottish_clan_map.png" width="170" height="223" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Scottish_clan_map.png/255px-Scottish_clan_map.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Scottish_clan_map.png/340px-Scottish_clan_map.png 2x" data-file-width="1065" data-file-height="1400" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Scottish_clan_map.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map showing Highland clans and Lowland surnames</div></div></div>\n<p>The organisation of society is obscure in the early part of the period, for which there are few documentary sources.<sup id="cite_ref-Thronton2009_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thronton2009-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> Kinship probably provided the primary unit of organisation and society was divided between a small <a href="/wiki/Aristocracy" title="Aristocracy">aristocracy</a>, whose rationale was based around warfare,<sup id="cite_ref-Haigh1999pp82-4_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Haigh1999pp82-4-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> a wider group of freemen, who had the right to bear arms and were represented in law codes,<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> above a relatively large body of slaves, who may have lived beside and become clients of their owners.<sup id="cite_ref-Woolf2007pp17-20_94-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Woolf2007pp17-20-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> By the thirteenth century there are sources that allow greater stratification in society to be seen, with layers including the king and a small elite of mormaers above lesser ranks of freemen and what was probably a large group of <a href="/wiki/Serfs" class="mw-redirect" title="Serfs">serfs</a>, particularly in central Scotland.<sup id="cite_ref-Grant1993_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Grant1993-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> In this period the feudalism introduced under David I meant that baronial lordships began to overlay this system, the English terms <a href="/wiki/Earl" title="Earl">earl</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thane" title="Thane">thane</a> became widespread.<sup id="cite_ref-Grant1997_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Grant1997-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> Below the noble ranks were <a href="/wiki/Husbandmen" class="mw-redirect" title="Husbandmen">husbandmen</a> with small farms and growing numbers of <a href="/wiki/Cottar" class="mw-redirect" title="Cottar">cottars</a> and gresemen with more modest landholdings.<sup id="cite_ref-Barrow1995_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barrow1995-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The combination of <a href="/wiki/Agnatic" class="mw-redirect" title="Agnatic">agnatic</a> kinship and feudal obligations has been seen as creating the system of <a href="/wiki/Scottish_clans" class="mw-redirect" title="Scottish clans">clans</a> in the Highlands in this era.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> Scottish society adopted theories of the <a href="/wiki/Three_estates" class="mw-redirect" title="Three estates">three estates</a> to describe its society and English terminology to differentiate ranks.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> Serfdom disappeared from the records in the fourteenth century<sup id="cite_ref-Goodacre1999pp57-60_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Goodacre1999pp57-60-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> and new social groups of labourers, craftsmen and merchants, became important in the developing <a href="/wiki/Burgh" title="Burgh">burghs</a>. This led to increasing social tensions in urban society, but, in contrast to England and France, there was a lack of major unrest in Scottish rural society, where there was relatively little economic change.<sup id="cite_ref-Wormald1991pp48-9_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wormald1991pp48-9-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Culture">Culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_Scotland" title="Culture of Scotland">Culture of Scotland</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Language_and_culture">Language and culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Language and culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Celtic_languages" title="Celtic languages">Celtic languages</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Languages_of_Scotland_1400_AD.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Languages_of_Scotland_1400_AD.svg/170px-Languages_of_Scotland_1400_AD.svg.png" width="170" height="259" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Languages_of_Scotland_1400_AD.svg/255px-Languages_of_Scotland_1400_AD.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Languages_of_Scotland_1400_AD.svg/340px-Languages_of_Scotland_1400_AD.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="932" data-file-height="1418" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Languages_of_Scotland_1400_AD.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An interpretation of the linguistic divide c. 1400, based on place-name evidence.<div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #357EC7; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Gaelic</div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #ff0; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Scots</div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #F87217; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Norn_language" title="Norn language">Norn</a></div></div></div></div>\n<p>Modern linguists divide Celtic languages into two major groups, the <a href="/wiki/P-Celtic" class="mw-redirect" title="P-Celtic">P-Celtic</a>, from which the <a href="/wiki/Brythonic_languages" class="mw-redirect" title="Brythonic languages">Brythonic languages</a>: <a href="/wiki/Welsh_language" title="Welsh language">Welsh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Breton_language" title="Breton language">Breton</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cornish_language" title="Cornish language">Cornish</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cumbric" title="Cumbric">Cumbric</a> derive, and the <a href="/wiki/Q-Celtic" class="mw-redirect" title="Q-Celtic">Q-Celtic</a>, from which come the <a href="/wiki/Goidelic_languages" title="Goidelic languages">Goidelic languages</a>: <a href="/wiki/Irish_language" title="Irish language">Irish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Manx_language" title="Manx language">Manx</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gaelic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Gaelic language">Gaelic</a>. The Pictish language remains enigmatic, since the Picts had no written script of their own and all that survives are place names and some isolated inscriptions in Irish <a href="/wiki/Ogham" title="Ogham">ogham</a> script.<sup id="cite_ref-Haywoodp2004p116_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Haywoodp2004p116-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> Most modern linguists accept that, although the nature and unity of Pictish language is unclear, it belonged to the former group.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> Historical sources, as well as place name evidence, indicate the ways in which the Pictish language in the north and Cumbric languages in the south were overlaid and replaced by Gaelic, <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">Old English</a> and later <a href="/wiki/Norse_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Norse language">Norse</a> in this period.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> By the High Middle Ages the majority of people within Scotland spoke the Gaelic language, then simply called <i>Scottish</i>, or in Latin, <i>lingua Scotica</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> The Kingdom of Alba was overwhelmingly an oral society dominated by Gaelic culture. Our fuller sources for Ireland of the same period suggest that there would have been <a href="/wiki/Filidh" class="mw-redirect" title="Filidh">filidh</a>, who acted as poets, musicians and historians, often attached to the court of a lord or king, and passed on their knowledge and culture in Gaelic to the next generation.<sup id="cite_ref-Crawford2009_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Crawford2009-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Houston2002p76_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Houston2002p76-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the Northern Isles the Norse language brought by Scandinavian occupiers and settlers evolved into the local <a href="/wiki/Norn_language" title="Norn language">Norn</a>, which lingered until the end of the eighteenth century<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> and Norse may also have survived as a spoken language until the sixteenth century in the <a href="/wiki/Outer_Hebrides" title="Outer Hebrides">Outer Hebrides</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> French, <a href="/wiki/Flemish" title="Flemish">Flemish</a> and particularly English became the main language of Scottish burghs, most of which were located in the south and east, an area to which Anglian settlers had already brought a form of Old English. In the later part of the twelfth century, the writer <a href="/wiki/Adam_of_Dryburgh" title="Adam of Dryburgh">Adam of Dryburgh</a> described lowland Lothian as "the Land of the English in the Kingdom of the Scots".<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> At least from the accession of David I, Gaelic ceased to be the main language of the royal court and was probably replaced by French, as evidenced by reports from contemporary chronicles, literature and translations of administrative documents into the French language. After this "de-gallicisation" of the Scottish court, a less highly regarded order of <a href="/wiki/Bard" title="Bard">bards</a> took over the functions of the filidh and they would continue to act in a similar role in the Highlands and Islands into the eighteenth century. They often trained in bardic schools, of which a few, like the one run by the <a href="/wiki/MacMhuirich_bardic_family" title="MacMhuirich bardic family">MacMhuirich</a> dynasty, who were bards to the <a href="/wiki/Lord_of_the_Isles" title="Lord of the Isles">Lord of the Isles</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> existed in Scotland and a larger number in Ireland, until they were suppressed from the seventeenth century.<sup id="cite_ref-Houston2002p76_122-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Houston2002p76-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> Members of bardic schools were trained in the complex rules and forms of Gaelic poetry.<sup id="cite_ref-Wormald1991pp60-7_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wormald1991pp60-7-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> Much of their work was never written down and what survives was only recorded from the sixteenth century.<sup id="cite_ref-Crawford2009_121-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Crawford2009-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the late Middle Ages, <a href="/wiki/Middle_Scots" title="Middle Scots">Middle Scots</a>, often simply called English, became the dominant language of the country. It was derived largely from Old English, with the addition of elements from Gaelic and French. Although resembling the language spoken in northern England, it became a distinct dialect from the late fourteenth century onwards.<sup id="cite_ref-Wormald1991pp60-7_127-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wormald1991pp60-7-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> It began to be adopted by the ruling elite as they gradually abandoned French. By the fifteenth century it was the language of government, with acts of parliament, council records and treasurer\'s accounts almost all using it from the reign of James I onwards. As a result, Gaelic, once dominant north of the Tay, began a steady decline.<sup id="cite_ref-Wormald1991pp60-7_127-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wormald1991pp60-7-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> Lowland writers began to treat Gaelic as a second class, rustic and even amusing language, helping to frame attitudes towards the highlands and to create a cultural gulf with the lowlands.<sup id="cite_ref-Wormald1991pp60-7_127-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wormald1991pp60-7-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Education">Education</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Education">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Education_in_Medieval_Scotland" title="Education in Medieval Scotland">Education in Medieval Scotland</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Tower_of_St._Salvator%27s_College,_St._Andrews_Fife.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Tower_of_St._Salvator%27s_College%2C_St._Andrews_Fife.jpg/170px-Tower_of_St._Salvator%27s_College%2C_St._Andrews_Fife.jpg" width="170" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Tower_of_St._Salvator%27s_College%2C_St._Andrews_Fife.jpg/255px-Tower_of_St._Salvator%27s_College%2C_St._Andrews_Fife.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Tower_of_St._Salvator%27s_College%2C_St._Andrews_Fife.jpg/340px-Tower_of_St._Salvator%27s_College%2C_St._Andrews_Fife.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1920" data-file-height="2560" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Tower_of_St._Salvator%27s_College,_St._Andrews_Fife.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Tower of <a href="/wiki/St_Salvator%27s_College,_St_Andrews" title="St Salvator&#39;s College, St Andrews">St Salvator\'s College, St Andrews</a>, one of the three universities founded in the fifteenth century</div></div></div>\n<p>The establishment of Christianity brought Latin to Scotland as a scholarly and written language. Monasteries served as major repositories of knowledge and education, often running schools and providing a small educated elite, who were essential to create and read documents in a largely illiterate society.<sup id="cite_ref-Macquarrie2004p128_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Macquarrie2004p128-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> In the High Middle Ages new sources of education arose, with <a href="/wiki/Choir_school" class="mw-redirect" title="Choir school">song</a> and <a href="/wiki/Grammar_school" title="Grammar school">grammar schools</a>. These were usually attached to cathedrals or a <a href="/wiki/Collegiate_church" title="Collegiate church">collegiate church</a> and were most common in the developing burghs. By the end of the Middle Ages grammar schools could be found in all the main burghs and some small towns. Early examples including the <a href="/wiki/High_School_of_Glasgow" title="High School of Glasgow">High School of Glasgow</a> in 1124 and the <a href="/wiki/High_School_of_Dundee" title="High School of Dundee">High School of Dundee</a> in 1239.<sup id="cite_ref-Bawcutt&amp;Williams2006pp29-30_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bawcutt&amp;Williams2006pp29-30-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> There were also petty schools, more common in rural areas and providing an elementary education.<sup id="cite_ref-Lynchpp104-7_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lynchpp104-7-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup> Some monasteries, like the Cistercian <a href="/wiki/Kinloss_Abbey" title="Kinloss Abbey">abbey at Kinloss</a>, opened their doors to a wider range of students.<sup id="cite_ref-Lynchpp104-7_130-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lynchpp104-7-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup> The number and size of these schools seems to have expanded rapidly from the 1380s. They were almost exclusively aimed at boys, but by the end of the fifteenth century, Edinburgh also had schools for girls, sometimes described as "sewing schools", and probably taught by lay women or nuns.<sup id="cite_ref-Bawcutt&amp;Williams2006pp29-30_129-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bawcutt&amp;Williams2006pp29-30-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Lynchpp104-7_130-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lynchpp104-7-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup> There was also the development of private tuition in the families of lords and wealthy burghers.<sup id="cite_ref-Bawcutt&amp;Williams2006pp29-30_129-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bawcutt&amp;Williams2006pp29-30-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> The growing emphasis on education cumulated with the passing of the <a href="/wiki/Education_Act_1496" title="Education Act 1496">Education Act 1496</a>, which decreed that all sons of barons and freeholders of substance should attend grammar schools to learn "perfyct Latyne". All this resulted in an increase in literacy, but which was largely concentrated among a male and wealthy elite,<sup id="cite_ref-Bawcutt&amp;Williams2006pp29-30_129-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bawcutt&amp;Williams2006pp29-30-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> with perhaps 60 per cent of the nobility being literate by the end of the period.<sup id="cite_ref-Wormald1991pp68-72_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wormald1991pp68-72-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Until the fifteenth century, those who wished to attend university had to travel to England or the continent, and just over a 1,000 have been identified as doing so between the twelfth century and 1410.<sup id="cite_ref-Websterpp124-5_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Websterpp124-5-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> Among these the most important intellectual figure was <a href="/wiki/John_Duns_Scotus" class="mw-redirect" title="John Duns Scotus">John Duns Scotus</a>, who studied at <a href="/wiki/University_of_Oxford" title="University of Oxford">Oxford</a>, <a href="/wiki/University_of_Cambridge" title="University of Cambridge">Cambridge</a> and <a href="/wiki/University_of_Paris" title="University of Paris">Paris</a> and probably died at <a href="/wiki/University_of_Cologne" title="University of Cologne">Cologne</a> in 1308, becoming a major influence on late Medieval religious thought.<sup id="cite_ref-Websterpp119_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Websterpp119-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> After the outbreak of the Wars of Independence, with occasional exceptions under safe conduct, English universities were closed to Scots and continental universities became more significant.<sup id="cite_ref-Websterpp124-5_132-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Websterpp124-5-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> Some Scottish scholars became teachers in continental universities. At Paris this included John De Rate and <a href="/wiki/Walter_Wardlaw" title="Walter Wardlaw">Walter Wardlaw</a> in the 1340s and 1350s, William de Tredbrum in the 1380s and Laurence de Lindores in the early 1500s.<sup id="cite_ref-Websterpp124-5_132-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Websterpp124-5-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> This situation was transformed by the founding of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_St_Andrews" title="University of St Andrews">University of St Andrews</a> in 1413, the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Glasgow" title="University of Glasgow">University of Glasgow</a> in 1450 and the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Aberdeen" title="University of Aberdeen">University of Aberdeen</a> in 1495.<sup id="cite_ref-Bawcutt&amp;Williams2006pp29-30_129-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bawcutt&amp;Williams2006pp29-30-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> Initially these institutions were designed for the training of clerics, but they would increasingly be used by laymen who would begin to challenge the clerical monopoly of administrative post in the government and law. Those wanting to study for second degrees still needed to go elsewhere and Scottish scholars continued to visit the continent and English universities reopened to Scots in the late fifteenth century.<sup id="cite_ref-Websterpp124-5_132-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Websterpp124-5-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> The continued movement to other universities produced a school of Scottish <a href="/wiki/Nominalists" class="mw-redirect" title="Nominalists">nominalists</a> at Paris in the early sixteenth century, of which <a href="/wiki/John_Major_(philosopher)" title="John Major (philosopher)">John Mair</a> was probably the most important figure. He had probably studied at a Scottish grammar school, then Cambridge, before moving to Paris, where he matriculated in 1493. By 1497 the humanist and historian <a href="/wiki/Hector_Boece" title="Hector Boece">Hector Boece</a>, born in Dundee and who had studied at Paris, returned to become the first principal at the new university of Aberdeen.<sup id="cite_ref-Websterpp124-5_132-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Websterpp124-5-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> These international contacts helped integrate Scotland into a wider European scholarly world and would be one of the most important ways in which the new ideas of <a href="/wiki/Humanism" title="Humanism">humanism</a> were brought into Scottish intellectual life.<sup id="cite_ref-Wormald1991pp68-72_131-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wormald1991pp68-72-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Literature">Literature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Literature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Scottish_literature_in_the_Middle_Ages" title="Scottish literature in the Middle Ages">Scottish literature in the Middle Ages</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Gododdin1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Gododdin1.jpg/170px-Gododdin1.jpg" width="170" height="229" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Gododdin1.jpg/255px-Gododdin1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Gododdin1.jpg/340px-Gododdin1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="969" data-file-height="1305" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Gododdin1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A page from the <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Aneirin" title="Book of Aneirin">Book of Aneirin</a> shows the first part of the text from the <a href="/wiki/Y_Gododdin" title="Y Gododdin">Y Gododdin</a>, c. sixth century</div></div></div>\n<p>Much of the earliest <a href="/wiki/Welsh-language_literature" title="Welsh-language literature">Welsh literature</a> was actually composed in or near the country now called Scotland, in the Brythonic speech, from which <a href="/wiki/Welsh_language" title="Welsh language">Welsh</a> would be derived, include <i>The <a href="/wiki/Y_Gododdin" title="Y Gododdin">Gododdin</a></i> and the <i><a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Gwen_Ystrad" class="mw-redirect" title="Battle of Gwen Ystrad">Battle of Gwen Ystrad</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> There are also religious works in <a href="/wiki/Middle_Irish" title="Middle Irish">Gaelic</a> including the <i>Elegy for <a href="/wiki/St_Columba" class="mw-redirect" title="St Columba">St Columba</a></i> by Dallan Forgaill, c. 597 and "In Praise of St Columba" by Beccan mac Luigdech of Rum, c. 677.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Latin_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Latin language">Latin</a> they include a "Prayer for Protection" (attributed to St Mugint), c. mid-sixth century and <i>Altus Prosator</i> ("The High Creator", attributed to St Columba), c. 597.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> In Old English there is <i>The <a href="/wiki/Dream_of_the_Rood" title="Dream of the Rood">Dream of the Rood</a></i>, from which lines are found on the <a href="/wiki/Ruthwell_Cross" title="Ruthwell Cross">Ruthwell Cross</a>, making it the only surviving fragment of <a href="/wiki/Northumbrian_(Anglo-Saxon)" class="mw-redirect" title="Northumbrian (Anglo-Saxon)">Northumbrian</a> <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">Old English</a> from early Medieval Scotland.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup> Before the reign of David I, the Scots possessed a flourishing literary elite that produced texts in both Gaelic and Latin, a tradition that survived in the Highlands into the thirteenth century.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> It is possible that more Middle Irish literature was written in Medieval Scotland than is often thought, but has not survived because the Gaelic literary establishment of eastern Scotland died out before the fourteenth century.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> In the thirteenth century, <a href="/wiki/Old_French_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Old French language">French</a> flourished as a <a href="/wiki/Literary_language" title="Literary language">literary language</a>, and produced the <i><a href="/wiki/Roman_de_Fergus" title="Roman de Fergus">Roman de Fergus</a></i>, the earliest piece of non-Celtic <a href="/wiki/Vernacular" title="Vernacular">vernacular</a> literature to survive from Scotland.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The first surviving major text in <a href="/wiki/Early_Scots" title="Early Scots">Early Scots</a> literature is <a href="/wiki/John_Barbour_(poet)" title="John Barbour (poet)">John Barbour</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Brus" title="The Brus">Brus</a></i> (1375), composed under the patronage of Robert II and telling the story in epic poetry of Robert I\'s actions before the English invasion till the end of the war of independence.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> Much Middle Scots literature was produced by <a href="/wiki/Makars" class="mw-redirect" title="Makars">makars</a>, poets with links to the royal court, which included James I (who wrote <i><a href="/wiki/The_Kingis_Quair" title="The Kingis Quair">The Kingis Quair</a></i>). Many of the makars had a university education and so were also connected with the <a href="/wiki/Ecclesia_(church)" class="mw-redirect" title="Ecclesia (church)">Kirk</a>. However, Dunbar\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Lament_for_the_Makaris" title="Lament for the Makaris">Lament for the Makaris</a></i> (c.1505) provides evidence of a wider tradition of secular writing outside of Court and Kirk, now largely lost.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup> Before the advent of printing in Scotland, writers such as <a href="/wiki/Robert_Henryson" title="Robert Henryson">Robert Henryson</a>, <a href="/wiki/William_Dunbar" title="William Dunbar">William Dunbar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Walter_Kennedy_(poet)" title="Walter Kennedy (poet)">Walter Kennedy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gavin_Douglas" title="Gavin Douglas">Gavin Douglas</a> have been seen as leading a golden age in Scottish poetry.<sup id="cite_ref-Wormald1991pp60-7_127-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wormald1991pp60-7-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> In the late fifteenth century, Scots prose also began to develop as a genre. Although there are earlier fragments of original Scots prose, such as the <i>Auchinleck Chronicle</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> the first complete surviving work includes <a href="/wiki/John_Ireland_(theologian)" title="John Ireland (theologian)">John Ireland</a>\'s <i>The Meroure of Wyssdome</i> (1490).<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup> There were also prose translations of French books of chivalry that survive from the 1450s, including <i>The Book of the Law of Armys</i> and the <i>Order of Knychthode</i> and the treatise <i><a href="/wiki/Secretum_Secretorum" title="Secretum Secretorum">Secreta Secetorum</a></i>, an Arabic work believed to be Aristotle\'s advice to <a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great" title="Alexander the Great">Alexander the Great</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Wormald1991pp60-7_127-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wormald1991pp60-7-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> The landmark work in the reign of James IV was <a href="/wiki/Gavin_Douglas" title="Gavin Douglas">Gavin Douglas</a>\'s version of <a href="/wiki/Virgil" title="Virgil">Virgil</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Aeneid" title="Aeneid">Aeneid</a></i>, the <i><a href="/wiki/Eneados" title="Eneados">Eneados</a></i>, which was the first complete translation of a major classical text in an <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">Anglian</a> language, finished in 1513, but overshadowed by the disaster at Flodden.<sup id="cite_ref-Wormald1991pp60-7_127-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wormald1991pp60-7-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Art">Art</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Art">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Art_in_Medieval_Scotland" title="Art in Medieval Scotland">Art in Medieval Scotland</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Trinity_Altarpiece.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Trinity_Altarpiece.jpg/220px-Trinity_Altarpiece.jpg" width="220" height="106" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Trinity_Altarpiece.jpg/330px-Trinity_Altarpiece.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Trinity_Altarpiece.jpg/440px-Trinity_Altarpiece.jpg 2x" data-file-width="540" data-file-height="260" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Trinity_Altarpiece.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Trinity_Altarpiece" title="Trinity Altarpiece">Trinity Altarpiece</a>, attributed to Flemish artist <a href="/wiki/Hugo_van_der_Goes" title="Hugo van der Goes">Hugo van der Goes</a> for the <a href="/wiki/Trinity_College_Kirk" title="Trinity College Kirk">Trinity College Kirk</a> in Edinburgh, Scotland, in the late fifteenth century</div></div></div>\n<p>In the early middles ages, there were distinct material cultures evident in the different linguistic groups, federations and kingdoms within what is now Scotland. Pictish art can be seen in the extensive survival of carved stones, particularly in the north and east of the country, which hold a variety of recurring images and patterns, as at <a href="/wiki/Dunrobin_Castle" title="Dunrobin Castle">Dunrobin</a> (<a href="/wiki/Sutherland" title="Sutherland">Sutherland</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Aberlemno" title="Aberlemno">Aberlemno</a> stones (<a href="/wiki/Angus,_Scotland" title="Angus, Scotland">Angus</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-Graham-Campbell1998pp.7-8_145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Graham-Campbell1998pp.7-8-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> It can also be seen in elaborate metal work that largely survives in buried hordes like the <a href="/wiki/St_Ninian%27s_Isle" title="St Ninian&#39;s Isle">St Ninian\'s Isle</a> Treasure.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> Irish-Scots art from the Kingdom of <a href="/wiki/D%C3%A1l_Riata" title="D\xc3\xa1l Riata">D\xc3\xa1l Riata</a> is much more difficult to identify, but may include items like the <a href="/wiki/Hunterston_brooch" class="mw-redirect" title="Hunterston brooch">Hunterston brooch</a>, which with other items like the <a href="/wiki/Monymusk_Reliquary" title="Monymusk Reliquary">Monymusk Reliquary</a>, suggest that D\xc3\xa1l Riata was one of the places, as a crossroads between cultures, where the <a href="/wiki/Insular_art" title="Insular art">Insular style</a> developed.<sup id="cite_ref-Lane2001_147-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lane2001-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup> Insular art is the name given to the common style that developed in Britain and Ireland after the conversion of the Picts and the cultural assimilation of Pictish culture into that of the Scots and Angles,<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup> and which became highly influential in continental Europe, contributing to the development of <a href="/wiki/Romanesque_art" title="Romanesque art">Romanesque</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gothic_art" title="Gothic art">Gothic</a> styles.<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup> It can be seen in elaborate jewellery, often making extensive use of semi-precious stones,<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup> in the heavily carved <a href="/wiki/High_cross" title="High cross">High crosses</a> found most frequently in the Highlands and Islands, but distributed across the country<sup id="cite_ref-Koch2006pp915-19_151-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Koch2006pp915-19-151">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup> and particularly in the highly decorated illustrated manuscripts such as the <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Kells" title="Book of Kells">Book of Kells</a>, which may have been begun, or wholly created on Iona. The finest era of the style was brought to an end by the disruption to monastic centres and aristocratic life of the Viking raids in the late eighth century.<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>\nScotland adopted the Romanesque in the late twelfth century, retaining and reviving elements of its style after the Gothic had become dominant elsewhere from the thirteenth century.<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup> Much of the best Scottish artwork of the High and Late Middle Ages was either religious in nature or realised in metal and woodwork, and has not survived the impact of time and of the Reformation.<sup id="cite_ref-Webster1997pp127-9_154-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Webster1997pp127-9-154">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup> However, examples of sculpture are extant as part of church architecture, including evidence of elaborate church interiors like the sacrament houses at Deskford and Kinkell<sup id="cite_ref-Whyte&amp;Whyte1991p117_155-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Whyte&amp;Whyte1991p117-155">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup> and the carvings of the <a href="/wiki/Seven_deadly_sins" title="Seven deadly sins">seven deadly sins</a> at <a href="/wiki/Rosslyn_Chapel" title="Rosslyn Chapel">Rosslyn Chapel</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup> From the thirteenth century, there are relatively large numbers of monumental effigies like the elaborate Douglas tombs in the town of <a href="/wiki/Douglas,_South_Lanarkshire" title="Douglas, South Lanarkshire">Douglas</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup> Native craftsmanship can be seen in items like the <a href="/wiki/Bute_mazer" title="Bute mazer">Bute mazer</a> and the Savernake Horn, and more widely in the large number of high quality seals that survive from the mid thirteenth century onwards.<sup id="cite_ref-Webster1997pp127-9_154-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Webster1997pp127-9-154">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup> Visual illustration can be seen in the illumination of charters,<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup> and occasional survivals like the fifteenth century <a href="/wiki/Doom_painting" class="mw-redirect" title="Doom painting">Doom painting</a> at <a href="/wiki/Guthrie,_Angus" title="Guthrie, Angus">Guthrie</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup> Surviving copies of individual portraits are relatively crude, but more impressive are the works or artists commissioned from the continent, particularly the Netherlands, including <a href="/wiki/Hugo_van_Der_Goes" class="mw-redirect" title="Hugo van Der Goes">Hugo van Der Goes</a>\'s altarpiece for the <a href="/wiki/Trinity_College_Kirk" title="Trinity College Kirk">Trinity College Church in Edinburgh</a> and the <i><a href="/wiki/Hours_of_James_IV_of_Scotland" title="Hours of James IV of Scotland">Hours of James IV of Scotland</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Wormald1991pp57-9_160-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wormald1991pp57-9-160">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup></p><div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Architecture">Architecture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Architecture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Architecture_of_Scotland_in_the_Middle_Ages" title="Architecture of Scotland in the Middle Ages">Architecture of Scotland in the Middle Ages</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Am_linlithgow_palace_north_west.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Am_linlithgow_palace_north_west.jpg/220px-Am_linlithgow_palace_north_west.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Am_linlithgow_palace_north_west.jpg/330px-Am_linlithgow_palace_north_west.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Am_linlithgow_palace_north_west.jpg/440px-Am_linlithgow_palace_north_west.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2048" data-file-height="1536" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Am_linlithgow_palace_north_west.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Linlithgow_Palace" title="Linlithgow Palace">Linlithgow Palace</a>, the first building to bear that title in Scotland, extensively rebuilt along Renaissance principles from the fifteenth century</div></div></div>\n<p>Medieval <a href="/wiki/Vernacular_architecture" title="Vernacular architecture">vernacular architecture</a> utilised local building materials, including <a href="/wiki/Cruck" title="Cruck">cruck</a> constructed houses, turf walls and clay, with a heavy reliance on stone.<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup> As burghs developed there were more sophisticated houses for the nobles, burgesses and other inhabitants.<sup id="cite_ref-MacQuarrie2004pp136-40_100-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MacQuarrie2004pp136-40-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> By the end of the period some were stone built with slate roofs or tiles.<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup> Medieval parish church architecture was typically simpler than in England, with many churches remaining simple oblongs, without <a href="/wiki/Transept" title="Transept">transepts</a> and <a href="/wiki/Aisle" title="Aisle">aisles</a>, and often without towers.<sup id="cite_ref-Whyte&amp;Whyte1991p117_155-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Whyte&amp;Whyte1991p117-155">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup> From the eleventh century there were influences from English and continental European designs and grander ecclesiastical buildings were built in the <a href="/wiki/Romanesque_architecture" title="Romanesque architecture">Romanesque</a> style, as can be seen at <a href="/wiki/Dunfermline_Abbey" title="Dunfermline Abbey">Dunfermline Abbey</a> and <a href="/wiki/Elgin_Cathedral" title="Elgin Cathedral">Elgin Cathedral</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Maxwell2005pp22-3_163-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Maxwell2005pp22-3-163">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup> and later the <a href="/wiki/Gothic_architecture" title="Gothic architecture">Gothic</a> style as at <a href="/wiki/Glasgow_Cathedral" title="Glasgow Cathedral">Glasgow Cathedral</a> and in the rebuilding of <a href="/wiki/Melrose_Abbey" title="Melrose Abbey">Melrose Abbey</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Wormald1991pp57-9_160-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wormald1991pp57-9-160">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup> From the early fifteenth century the introduction of Renaissance styles included the selective return of <a href="/wiki/Romanesque_architecture" title="Romanesque architecture">Romanesque</a> forms, as in the nave of <a href="/wiki/Dunkeld_Cathedral" title="Dunkeld Cathedral">Dunkeld Cathedral</a> and in the chapel of Bishop Elphinstone\'s <a href="/wiki/Kings_College,_Aberdeen" class="mw-redirect" title="Kings College, Aberdeen">Kings College, Aberdeen</a> (1500\xe2\x80\x9309).<sup id="cite_ref-Glendinningetal1996pp3-4_164-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Glendinningetal1996pp3-4-164">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup> Many of the motte and bailey castles introduced into Scotland with feudalism in the twelfth century and the castles "<a href="/wiki/Enceinte" title="Enceinte">enceinte</a>", with a high <a href="/wiki/Battlements" class="mw-redirect" title="Battlements">embattled</a> curtain wall that replaced those still in occupation, were slighted during the Wars of Independence. In the late Middle Ages new castles were built, some on a grander scale as "<a href="/wiki/Livery_and_maintenance" class="mw-redirect" title="Livery and maintenance">livery and maintenance</a>" castles, to house retained troops.<sup id="cite_ref-West1985p26_66-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-West1985p26-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Gunpowder" title="Gunpowder">Gunpowder</a> weaponry fundamentally altered the nature of castle architecture, with existing castles being adapted to allow the use of gunpowder weapons by the incorporation of "keyhole" gun ports, platforms to mount guns and walls being adapted to resist bombardment. <a href="/wiki/Ravenscraig_Castle" title="Ravenscraig Castle">Ravenscraig</a>, Kirkcaldy, begun about 1460, is probably the first castle in the British Isles to be built as an <a href="/wiki/Artillery" title="Artillery">artillery</a> fort, incorporating "D-shape" bastions that would better resist cannon fire and on which artillery could be mounted.<sup id="cite_ref-West1985p27_70-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-West1985p27-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> The largest number of late medieval fortifications in Scotland built by nobles were of the <a href="/wiki/Tower_house" title="Tower house">tower house</a> design.<sup id="cite_ref-Stell2000p278_165-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stell2000p278-165">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Reid2006p12_166-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reid2006p12-166">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup> primarily aimed to provide protection against smaller raiding parties, rather than a major siege.<sup id="cite_ref-Reid2006p33_167-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reid2006p33-167">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup> Extensive building and rebuilding of royal palaces in the Renaissance style probably began under <a href="/wiki/James_III_of_Scotland" title="James III of Scotland">James III</a> and accelerated under <a href="/wiki/James_IV_of_Scotland" title="James IV of Scotland">James IV</a>. <a href="/wiki/Linlithgow_Palace" title="Linlithgow Palace">Linlithgow</a> was first constructed under James I, under the direction of master of work John de Waltoun and was referred to as a palace, apparently the first use of this term in the country, from 1429. This was extended under James III and began to correspond to a fashionable quadrangular, corner-towered Italian signorial palace, combining classical symmetry with neo-chivalric imagery.<sup id="cite_ref-Glendinningetal1996p9_168-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Glendinningetal1996p9-168">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Music">Music</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Music">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Music_in_Medieval_Scotland" title="Music in Medieval Scotland">Music in Medieval Scotland</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Stirling_Castle_Chapel_Royal_interior.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Stirling_Castle_Chapel_Royal_interior.jpg/220px-Stirling_Castle_Chapel_Royal_interior.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Stirling_Castle_Chapel_Royal_interior.jpg/330px-Stirling_Castle_Chapel_Royal_interior.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Stirling_Castle_Chapel_Royal_interior.jpg/440px-Stirling_Castle_Chapel_Royal_interior.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3648" data-file-height="2736" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Stirling_Castle_Chapel_Royal_interior.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Chapel_Royal,_Stirling_Castle" class="mw-redirect" title="Chapel Royal, Stirling Castle">Chapel Royal, Stirling Castle</a>, a major focus for liturgical music</div></div></div><p>\nIn the late twelfth century, <a href="/wiki/Giraldus_Cambrensis" class="mw-redirect" title="Giraldus Cambrensis">Giraldus Cambrensis</a> noted that "in the opinion of many, Scotland not only equals its teacher, Ireland, but indeed greatly outdoes it and excels her in musical skill". He identified the Scots as using the <a href="/wiki/Cithara" title="Cithara">cithara</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tympanum_(hand_drum)" title="Tympanum (hand drum)">tympanum</a> and chorus, although what exactly these instruments were is unclear.<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup> Bards probably accompanied their poetry on the <a href="/wiki/Harp" title="Harp">harp</a>, and can also be seen in records of the Scottish courts throughout the Medieval period.<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup> Scottish church music from the thirteenth century was increasingly influenced by continental developments, with figures like the musical theorist Simon Tailler studying in Paris, before returned to Scotland where he introduced several reforms of church music.<sup id="cite_ref-Elliott1973_171-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Elliott1973-171">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup> Scottish collections of music like the thirteenth century \'Wolfenb\xc3\xbcttel 677\', which is associated with <a href="/wiki/St_Andrews" title="St Andrews">St Andrews</a>, contain mostly French compositions, but with some distinctive local styles.<sup id="cite_ref-Elliott1973_171-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Elliott1973-171">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup> The captivity of James I in England from 1406 to 1423, where he earned a reputation as a poet and composer, may have led him to take English and continental styles and musicians back to the Scottish court on his release.<sup id="cite_ref-Elliott1973_171-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Elliott1973-171">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup> In the late fifteenth century a series of Scottish musicians trained in the Netherlands before returning home, including John Broune, Thomas Inglis and John Fety, the last of whom became master of the song school in Aberdeen and then Edinburgh, introducing the new five-fingered organ playing technique.<sup id="cite_ref-Wormald1991_172-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wormald1991-172">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup> In 1501 James IV refounded the Chapel Royal within <a href="/wiki/Stirling_Castle" title="Stirling Castle">Stirling Castle</a>, with a new and enlarged choir and it became the focus of Scottish liturgical music. Burgundian and English influences were probably reinforced when Henry VII\'s daughter Margaret Tudor married James IV in 1503.<sup id="cite_ref-Gosman2003_173-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gosman2003-173">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup></p><div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="National_identity">National identity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: National identity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Scottish_national_identity" title="Scottish national identity">Scottish national identity</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Royal_Banner_of_Scotland.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Royal_Banner_of_Scotland.svg/220px-Royal_Banner_of_Scotland.svg.png" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Royal_Banner_of_Scotland.svg/330px-Royal_Banner_of_Scotland.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Royal_Banner_of_Scotland.svg/440px-Royal_Banner_of_Scotland.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="375" data-file-height="250" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Royal_Banner_of_Scotland.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Royal Standard of Scotland, first adopted by king <a href="/wiki/William_I_of_Scotland" class="mw-redirect" title="William I of Scotland">William I</a> (1143\xe2\x80\x931214)</div></div></div>\n<p>In the High Middle Ages the word "Scot" was only used by Scots to describe themselves to foreigners, amongst whom it was the most common word. They called themselves <i>Albanach</i> or simply <i>Gaidel</i>. Both "Scot" and <i>Gaidel</i> were ethnic terms that connected them to the majority of the inhabitants of Ireland. At the beginning of the thirteenth century, the author of <i><a href="/wiki/De_Situ_Albanie" title="De Situ Albanie">De Situ Albanie</a></i> noted that: "The name Arregathel [Argyll] means margin of the Scots or Irish, because all Scots and Irish are generally called \'Gattheli\'."<sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup> Scotland came to possess a unity which transcended Gaelic, French and Germanic ethnic differences and by the end of the period, the Latin, French and English word "Scot" could be used for any subject of the Scottish king. Scotland\'s multilingual <a href="/wiki/Scoto-Norman" title="Scoto-Norman">Scoto-Norman</a> monarchs and mixed Gaelic and Scoto-Norman aristocracy all became part of the "Community of the Realm", in which ethnic differences were less divisive than in Ireland and Wales.<sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup> This identity was defined in opposition to English attempts to annexe the country and as a result of social and cultural changes. The resulting antipathy towards England dominated Scottish foreign policy well into the fifteenth century, making it extremely difficult for Scottish kings like James III and James IV to pursue policies of peace towards their southern neighbour.<sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup> In particular the <i>Declaration of Arbroath</i> asserted the ancient distinctiveness of Scotland in the face of English aggression, arguing that it was the role of the king to defend the independence of the community of Scotland. This document has been seen as the first "nationalist theory of sovereignty".<sup id="cite_ref-Kidd2003pp17-18_177-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kidd2003pp17-18-177">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_Scotland.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Flag_of_Scotland.svg/220px-Flag_of_Scotland.svg.png" width="220" height="132" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Flag_of_Scotland.svg/330px-Flag_of_Scotland.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Flag_of_Scotland.svg/440px-Flag_of_Scotland.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_Scotland.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Flag_of_Scotland" title="Flag of Scotland">Cross of St. Andrew</a>, adopted as a national symbol in this period</div></div></div><p>\nThe adoption of Middle Scots by the aristocracy has been seen as building a shared sense of national solidarity and culture between rulers and ruled, although the fact that north of the Tay Gaelic still dominated may have helped widen the cultural divide between highlands and lowlands.<sup id="cite_ref-Wormald1991pp55-7_178-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wormald1991pp55-7-178">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup> The national literature of Scotland created in the late medieval period employed legend and history in the service of the crown and nationalism, helping to foster a sense of national identity, at least within its elite audience. The epic poetic history of the <i>Brus</i> and <i>Wallace</i> helped outline a narrative of united struggle against the English enemy. Arthurian literature differed from conventional versions of the legend by treating <a href="/wiki/King_Arthur" title="King Arthur">Arthur</a> as a villain and <a href="/wiki/Mordred" title="Mordred">Mordred</a>, the son of the king of the Picts, as a hero.<sup id="cite_ref-Wormald1991pp55-7_178-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wormald1991pp55-7-178">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup> The origin myth of the Scots, systematised by <a href="/wiki/John_of_Fordun" title="John of Fordun">John of Fordun</a> (c. 1320-c. 1384), traced their beginnings from the Greek prince <a href="/wiki/Gathelus" class="mw-redirect" title="Gathelus">Gathelus</a> and his Egyptian wife <a href="/wiki/Scota" title="Scota">Scota</a>, allowing them to argue superiority over the English, who claimed their descent from the Trojans, who had been defeated by the Greeks.<sup id="cite_ref-Kidd2003pp17-18_177-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kidd2003pp17-18-177">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup> The image of St. Andrew, martyred while bound to an X-shaped cross, first appeared in the Scotland during the reign of William I and was again depicted on <a href="/wiki/Seal_(emblem)" title="Seal (emblem)">seals</a> used during the late thirteenth century; including on one particular example used by the <a href="/wiki/Guardians_of_Scotland" class="mw-redirect" title="Guardians of Scotland">Guardians of Scotland</a>, dated 1286.<sup id="cite_ref-NAS_179-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NAS-179">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup> Use of a simplified symbol associated with Saint Andrew, the <a href="/wiki/Saltire" title="Saltire">saltire</a>, has its origins in the late fourteenth century; the Parliament of Scotland decreed in 1385 that Scottish soldiers should wear a white Saint Andrew\'s Cross on their person, both in front and behind, for the purpose of identification. Use of a blue background for the Saint Andrew\'s Cross is said to date from at least the fifteenth century.<sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup> The earliest reference to the Saint Andrew\'s Cross as a flag is to be found in the <i>Vienna Book of Hours</i>, circa 1503.<sup id="cite_ref-Bartram_181-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bartram-181">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup></p><div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 35em; -webkit-column-width: 35em; column-width: 35em; list-style-type: decimal;">\n<ol class="references">\n<li id="cite_note-Haywoodp2004p116-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Haywoodp2004p116_1-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Haywoodp2004p116_1-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">J. Haywood, <i>The Celts: Bronze Age to New Age</i> (London: Pearson Education, 2004), <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r861714446">.mw-parser-output cite.citation{font-style:inherit}.mw-parser-output q{quotes:"\\"""\\"""\'""\'"}.mw-parser-output code.cs1-code{color:inherit;background:inherit;border:inherit;padding:inherit}.mw-parser-output .cs1-lock-free a{background:url("//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Lock-green.svg/9px-Lock-green.svg.png")no-repeat;background-position:right .1em center}.mw-parser-output .cs1-lock-limited a,.mw-parser-output .cs1-lock-registration a{background:url("//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Lock-gray-alt-2.svg/9px-Lock-gray-alt-2.svg.png")no-repeat;background-position:right .1em center}.mw-parser-output .cs1-lock-subscription a{background:url("//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Lock-red-alt-2.svg/9px-Lock-red-alt-2.svg.png")no-repeat;background-position:right .1em
